NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
APPRAISAL OF FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMIARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) has requested
Bank assistance to finance an agricultural development project in the North
Central State of the Federation. The project was identified by a Bank mission
in November 1972, and was subsequently prepared by the Bank in collaboration
with the North Central State authorities. This report is based on the find-
ings of an aopraisal mission that visited Nigeria in September and October
1973.
ii.       Under the present economic circumstances and associated industrial
growth, industry and the urban population are becoming more dependent on the
rural population for assured food and industrial crop supplies, and at the
same time the gap in income earnings between urban and rural communities is
becoming ever wider. The northern States of Nigeria and their rural popula-
tions are the main producers of grain crops for human consumption and of indus-
trial crops for processing industries, and yet their per capita income of
about US$40 per annum is the lowest in the Federation.
iii.      Except for the extreme northern areas, mainly north of 120 latitude,
the soils and climate of the northern States are generally suitable for ex-
tensive annual crop production. Research workers and the more responsive
farmers using improved inputs have adequately shown the response of improved
seeds to economic applications of fertilizers, and the viability of insecti-
cides for pest control purposes. Due to the disruptions caused by the civil
war (1966-68) and its aftermath, the lack of planning resources, and until
recently considerable financial constraints, very little had been done to
improve the production of the small farmer. Even now experienced manpower
to plan and implement rational developments in agriculture is in extremely
short supply. The Federal and State Governments recognizing the need to
increase the production from the small farm sector, 70% of the population,
now plan to accelerate the development of agriculture throughout Nigeria.
This project refers to one of three projects in northern Nigeria that the
Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) would develop with the Bank's
assistance.
iv.       The project covers the entire Funtua Division (7,500 km2) of
Katsina Province of the North Central State (10% of the State). The area
supports a population of about 905,000 people (16% of the State's popula-
tion) including some 100,000 families farming on average 3.9 ha each. The
net annual family income including the value of produce consumed on the fanm
is currently about US$266. The population is expanding, the soils are rapidly
declining in fertility, and the people are under constant pressure to increase
output. As such, conditions are ripe for project action and farmer response
is expected to be high.



- ii -
v.        The project would cover physical infrastructure, support services
and farm inputs; including 1,500 km of low cost agricultural roads; 85
earth dams and 160 ponds to provide water supplies to humans and livestock;
a project administrative center at Funtua, 5 development unit centers and
77 farm service centers; rehabilitate and equip two seed multiplication
farms to produce improved high yielding seed; and expand and create new training
facilities for project staff and farmers. The project would also provide bet-
ter support services in the form of a tenfold increase in extension staff,
introduce new credit and marketing services to the farmers; and the associated
staff houses, offices and transport facilities required to support these serv-
ices. The project would procure farm inputs to sell to farmers for cash or
credit.
vi.       A Federal operated Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU)
would be established to evaluate the operations and benefits of the proposed
investments. This unit would also evaluate other similar projects in northern
Nigeria.
vii.      The project would be managed by a special Management Unit responsi-
ble to an Executive Committee, both to be established under a gazetted order
of the North Central State Executive Council. The committee would be chaired
by the Permanent Secretary of the State's Ministry of Agriculture and Natural
Resources. The Management Unit would be suitably staffed through transfer
of existing Government staff or personnel presently under training. Defi-
ciencies in specialized technical skills and high level management staff,
unavailable in Nigeria, would be made up through international recruitment.
viii.     The proposed Bank loan of US$29 million would be to the Federal Military
Government of Nigeria for a term of 20 years, including a five-year grace
period. The loan would cover 51% of total project costs, estimated at
US$29.0 million. Of the US$29 million Bank loan, US$27.9 million would be
onlent to the North Central State Government (NCS), and US$1.1 million would
finance part of the cost of the PMEU. FMG would finance 1% of project cost
as its contribution to the PMEU evaluation section, US$0.7 million. NCS
would provide US$25.0 million or 43% of total project costs, project invest-
ment and operating costs. The remaining project costs estimated at US$2.9
million, or 5%, would be contributed by participating farmers in the form
of cash payments for seasonal inputs and down payments on farm equipment.
ix.       Procurement of vehicles, plant equipment, fertilizers, insecticides,
farm tractors and implements, having a value of more than US$25,000 would
be through international competitive bidding (ICB), and would have an esti-
mated value of US$20.6 million. Contracts for civil works such as houses,
offices and warehouses valued at US$7.5 million would not be suitable for
ICB, due to the dispersed locations and small size of individual contracts,
and would be awarded under locally advertised competitive bidding. Foreign
firms and suppliers who are well represented in Nigeria would not be pre-
cluded, but bonafide domestic contractors would receive a 7-1/2% preference
in bid evaluation. Construction of roads, water supplies and soil conserva-
tion works estimated at US$7.1 million would be carried out on force account
due to the shortage of suitable medium scale contractors who normally would



- iii -
undertake this work, however petty contractors would be employed to the
maximum extent feasible.   A large part of project costs, an estimated
US$15.5 million, would be for labor, staff salaries and allowances, vehicle
maintenance and operation, general services and project evaluation, and as
such would be unsuitable for competitive bidding. Internationally recruited
staff would be employed under terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank.
x.        Proceeds of the Bank loan would be disbursed to cover: (a) 100% of
the CIF costs of directly imported vehicles, tractors, heavy plant and
equipment, or 75% of the cost of these items if locally procured - US$2.4
million; (b) 100% of the CIF costs of directly imported farm tractors and
equipment, ox drawn equipment, spraying machines, fertilizers, insecticides,
battery sets and seeds, or 72% of these items if procured locally - US$6.7
million; (c) 100% of the cost of expatriate staff - US$1.8 million; (d)
60% of the cost of buildings, houses, furnishings, and construction mate-
rials and labor for roads, dams and soil conservation works - US$4.5 mil-
lion; (e) 60% of local staff costs - US$3.7 million; (f) 60% of the cost
of the evaluation section of PMEU and field evaluation units - US$1.4 mil-
lion; and (g) US$8.5 million to cover unallocated expenses. Disbursements
of the Bank loan would be against import documentation, contracts and certi-
fied records of expenditure, the latter would be scrutinized from time to
time by the Bank during supervision missions.
Xi.       The principal direct benefits would, through the output of 60,000
project farmers, be from project year 5 the annual incremental production
of 25,000 tons of seed cotton (115,000 bales of lint), 12,000 tons of ground-
nuts, 7,000 tons of sorghum, 61,000 tons of maize, and 2,500 tons of cowpeas;
the project would indirectly benefit a further 40,000 farm families through
provision of better communications, water supplies and markets. Including
the non-farming population, some 905,000 people would one way or other re-
ceive some benefit from the project, not least the project would provide
additional employment - equivalent to 12,000 manyears per annum. Intangible
benefits would include the provision of a basic infrastructure on which
health and other social services could be developed, expansion of local trade
to the non-farming population, and the strengthening of institutions including
the Local Authority and the State Vinistry of Agriculture and Natural Re-
sources. The project established evaluation unit would provide considerable
information to support the future planning of Federal and State agricultural
policies. The project would train Nigerian staff in management and technical
aspects applicable to rural development. The economic rate of return is 41%,
a 30% reduction in commodity prices would reduce the rate of return to 8% and
the project would still be viable (18% rate of return) if production targets
were reduced to 50% of those projected.  The net farm income for farmers
directly affected by the project would increase from US$266 to US$410 per
annum at full development, and the net per capita income of the total farming
population would rise from US$41 to US$63 inferring an annual income growth
rate of 8% per annum, compared to the present no growth situation, and is
achievable provided that the physical inputs and management needs are
provided.



- iv -
xii.      On the basis of the assurances that were obtained during negotia-
tion, and set out in Chapter IX, the project is suitable for a Bank loan
of US$29 million.



I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      This report aDpraises a loan application made to the Bank by the
Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG). The purpose of the loan would
be to assist Nigeria's North Central State Government (NCS) to finance an
agricultural development program involving 100,000 small farmers in the
Funtua Division of Katsina Province. The project would involve the construc-
tion of vital crop extraction roads and water supplies, and would provide
farmers with the technical, marketing, and credit services, and inputs needed
to insure significant increases in production and farmer incomes. Currently,
the latter are low, about US$266 per family, or US$41 per capita.
1.02      The project is deliberately large; this is essential in order to
make a meaningful impact in such a large and populous country. On the other
hand, the risks that do exist are minimized, provided competent management is
available, because the technical innovations proposed have been proven, the farmers
are hardworking, and the production induced by the project is in high demand
on domestic and export markets. The project concept could be applied through-
out most of northern Nigeria. One important role of the project would provide
a practical training ground for Nigerian staff in the technical and administra-
tive management of projects of this type.
1.03      The project is within the frame of policies outlined in Nigeria's
"Perspective Plan for Agricultural Development to 1985". In particular, it
follows FMG policies to increase production of both food and industrial crops
to supply Nigeria's rapidly increasing urban populations and industries includ-
ing intensive livestock production enterprises, particularly in the southern
States. The project would assist to develop sound agricultural credit systems
tailored to provide credit to large numbers of small farmers. It would provide
also a much needed base for the expansion and improvement of health and other
social services currently planned by the State Government; the project road
development program would be a vital first phase to facilitate the improve-
ment of these social services.
1.04      This report refers to similar projects at Gusau (NW State) and
Gombe (NE State). These projects were appraised concurrently and are similar
in nature; while administratively totally separate, they have common produc-
tion policies, techniques and services. The three projects represent a first,
but very substantial, step by the Federal and State Governments to increase
the production and raise the living standards of large numbers of peoples
in the relatively less developed and environmentally harsh northern States.



- 2 -
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      The 12-State Federal Republic of Nigeria has a total area of about
914,000 sq km, and in 1971, a population estimated at 68 million. About 35%
of GDP estimated at US$9.1 billion in 1972/3, is generated in the agricul-
ture sector, which employs 70% of the labor force. In 1972/3, total domestic
exports amounted to US$2.1 billion, of which petroleum represented about
82% (US$1.8 billion). The main agricultural exports are cocoa, groundnuts,
and groundnut oil and cake. Oil palm products used to be exported, but
with increasing domestic consumption and a general decline in the industry,
palm oil is no longer exported, and palm kernel exports are dwindling.
Cotton, once a major export, is now fully utilized by the local milling in-
dustry and local supply cannot meet demand. By and large, Nigeria is self-
sufficient in food; food imports are estimated at only US$2.6 per capita.
2.02      Nigeria can be divided into three major agricultural and ecological
zones (see map): a southern wet forest zone, south of the confluence of
the Benue and Niger Rivers, mainly growing perennial tree crops, including
cocoa, oil palm and rubber; a central dry forest zone known as the middle
belt which is underpopulated and tsetse infested, but has a high potential
for annual food crops and livestock; and a northern zone, covering about
50% of the country, with a tree savannah ecology, and which supports
Nigeria's cattle population, and produces its main cereal crops, and ground-
nuts and cotton.
B. Agriculture in Northern Nigeria
2.03      North Central State is one of four States in northern Nigeria
which have very similar agricultural and ecological systems. The four have
also a closely associated history of institutional development and agricul-
--J policies, as prior to 1968, the whole region, including two other
States, was administered as one from a regional capital at Kaduna. Thus,
a description of the North Central State, its policies and problems, would
apply closely to the situation in North Western, North Eastern and Kano
States.
2.04      North Central State, with a population of about 5.5 million, lie!
between latitudes 9-13° north and 6-9° east, and comprises about 70,000 km4
Within the State there is considerable climatic variation, and rainfall
varies from 1,250 mm in its south to less than 750 mm on the border with
Niger. Much of the State has an assured monomodal rainfall (4-5 months) of
at least 750 mm per annum; although the distribution within the total is
sometimes erratic, leading to "drought" years such as occurred in 1973.



- 3 -
Most of the State has a tree savannah ecology, and in those areas of higher
tree densities, the incidence of tsetse is high and trypanosomiasis is a
constraint to livestock production, as well as being a human health problem.
Consequently, a large proportion of the population occupies the healthier 2/3
of the State lying north of Kaduna. For these and for ethnic reasons, Katsina
Province has one of Nigeria's higher population densities (104 people per
km2), and farm sizes average less than 4 hectares. The proposed project which
would be centered on Funtua covers about 10% of the State and includes 16%
of the population.
2.05      The major crops are annuals such as millet and sorghum, the prin-
cipal cereals, and groundnuts, cotton, and cowpeas. Minor crops include maize,
tomatoes, chilies, cassava and yams. Livestock plays an important role in
the economy and include cattle, sheep, goats and poultry. The Fulani people,
who are nomadically inclined, own most of the livestock, and have a considera-
ble impact on the arable farmer. Traditionally, cotton and groundnuts have
been the north's main cash crops and are likely to remain so; but as the savan-
nah areas of Nigeria are generally well suited for cereal production, they could
play an important role in the future in producing cereals for the expanding
market created by intensive livestock industries and urban populations of the
south.
C. Agricultural Policy
2.06      Agricultural production in the State is virtually stagnant, and
any increase that is occurring is due to population growth. Although signi-
ficant improvements in crop yields have been demonstrated to be technically
and economically feasible by the research institutions, very little has been
done to help the farmer to obtain and use the innovations necessary to im-
prove his production. Until very recently, NCS had no clear-cut policy for
increasing production, and, in the absence of specific plans to implement
the policy, the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MTANR) still
tends to function on a basis of maintaining the status quo. The extension
service is weak and poorly staffed, credit and marketing institutions
barely exist, planning is poor, and the expertise needed to implement suc-
cessfullv major agricultural and rural development projects is in very short
supply.
2.07      Nigeria's manufacturing industries that are based on farm products
suclh as cotton and groundnuts, and the rapidly expanding urban population
(estimated to be increasing at 5% annually) are totally dependent on the
small traditional farmer for their raw materials and food supplies. These
farmers, as throughout Nigeria in general, are very poorly serviced, do not
use imDroved and drought resistant varieties and have very limited access
to fertilizers and agro-chemicals. Production is, therefore, irregular and
is governed by the vagaries of climate and other influences, such as pest
attack, that remain beyond the farmer's control. Given the foregoing, the
small farmer is unable to bank on producing an assured surplus.



- 4 -
2.08      FMG is becoming increasingly aware of the need to rectify the
current situation, and in discussions with the Bank and NCS, has agreed that
in the short term, at least, the quickest way of improving farm production and
incomes would be to provide a concentration of support services to areas of
reasonable potential and dense farm population. In such areas, farm sizes
cannot increase and farmers, with declining land fertility, are facing the
problem of maintaining, let alone increasing, production. Such farmers are
likely to respond well to any improvements that may be offered to them. For
the longer term, it will be important to develop programs to open up areas
of unused land, particularly in the now tsetse-infested middle belt, for
settlement by farmers and graziers.
2.09      The project appraised in this report would provide a package of
services to existing farming communities with the objective of increasing
production of cash and food crops; it would train agricultural, credit,
marketing, administrative and engineering staff, and would develop NCS's
agricultural planning capacity. The increase in farm incomes, generated
by project actions, would be significant. Further background information
is available in Report 416a-UNI titled "Current Economic Position and Prospects
of Nigeria" dated August 5, 1974 with special reference to Vol. III on "Problems
and Prospects of Food Production".
III. PROJECT AREA
A. General
3.01      The project area covers 7,500 km  or about 10% of the total area
of the North Central State. It comprises the whole of the Funtua Adminis-
trative Division of Katsina Province which is the southernmost Division of
Katsina emirate. Map shows area location within Nigeria.
3.02      The annual rainfall is about 1,100 mm and is confined entirely to
a 6-montlh neriod from April througlh September. Under reasonable management
and utilization of improved seeds and fertilizers, high yields can be
achieved under the prevalling climatic regime. Due to the seasonality of
rainfall and the underlying granitic basement complex, groundwater is severely
restricted, and with increasing land pressures stream flow has markedly
diminished and water supplies for human and livestock resources are a
limiting factor to development.
3.03      The project area is occupied by the people of the Hlausa tribe,
and bv mid-1974 is estimated to have some 100,000 farm families that support
a total project area population of 905,000. The social structure is signi-
ficant because of the relative strength and influence of the hierarchical
structure of the Local Authority. The project comes within the jurisdiction
of the Emir of Katsina whose hereditary authority is delegated through
salaried district and village heads. The latter are responsible for village
level administration of land tenure arrangements based on Islamic law and
on customary usufructory rights.



3.04      Information on farm size is scant but it is estimated that the
average size farm holdings is 3.9 ha. There are some large farms (over
100 ha) within the project area, but these are relatively few. Cropping
patterns (see Annex 1) are dominated by sorghum and millet as food grains,
and cotton and groundnuts as the major cash crops. Numerous minor crops
are grown.
3.05      The project area is well served by main roads, and has a railhead
at Funtua. However, crop extraction and other minor roads are virtually
non-existent, and communities are seasonally cut off from each other and the
outside world. Transportation of farmer produce and inputs is therefore dif-
ficult and expensive. Further project area data are at Annex 2.
B. Institutions
3.06      State Institutions that would be concerned with the project and
their relevant responsibilities are described in Annex 2. They include: the
Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR) that would have the
responsibility for carrying out the project; the Ministry of Works (MOW),
responsible for State-financed public works programs; the Ministry of Health
(MOH), responsible for all aspects of human health in the State; the Ministry
of Industry, Trade and Cooperatives (MITC) responsible for cooperative develop-
ment; and the Katsina Local Authority (KLA). The project area comes under the
control of the KLA, whose functions include the collection of tax in respect
of people and cattle (haraji and jangali, respectively); the administration
of land allocation; and the provision of services such as feeder road construc-
tion and maintenance, dispensaries, and primary education as a complement to
State Government activities in these fields.
3.07      Other institutions that would be concerned with or affected by the
project and which are described in Annex 2 would be the Funtua Cooperative
Credit and Marketing Union Ltd.; tlIe Rural Development Bureau (RDB), a State
Government body composed of representatives of public entities concerned with
the rural sector, and which has a relatively informal role in coordinating
the rural development aspects of State-sponsored programs; and the Institute of
Agricultural Research (IAR) (Annex 3) which was created in the early 1960's
from the Research Division of the then Northern Nigeria Ministry of Agriculture,
and which now is a semi-autonomous body linked with Ahmadu Bello University
(ABU).
3.08      Institutions concerned with crop marketing and processing are the
Northern States Mlarketing Board (NSMB) which as purchasing agent of the
Federal Nigerian Produce Marketing Company (NPMC) is responsible for organi-
zing the domestic purchase, through its Licensed Buying Agents (LBA), of
controlled crops such as cotton and groundnuts at prices predetermined by
FMG; and the Cotton and Agricultural Processors Ltd. (CAP) which was formerly
the British Cotton Growers' Association (Nigeria) Ltd. and which has had a
monopoly for ginning Nigerian cotton since exports began over half a century



ago. CAP has ginneries throughout the cotton-growing zones of the northern
States. The present ginning agreement between CAP and NSMB expires in 1976
and for this reason assurances were obtained from NCS during negotiations
that any future ginning arrangement would not adversely affect the production,
marketing or ginning of cotton within the project area. The Produce Inspec-
tion Division (PID) of MANR has important responsibilities for grading con-
trolled produce. Crop processing, marketing and grading arrangements are
described in Annex 2.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Description
4.01      The project would be carried out over a five-year investment period
1975/76-1979/80, and would involve:
-    constructing about 1,500 km of low cost agricultural roads,
85 earth dams, 160 tapkis (ponds), and associated soil con-
s ervation structures;
-    constructing an administrative center in Funtua, 5 develop-
ment unit centers, and 77 farm service centers;
-    rehabilitating Daudawa seed multiplication farm and the
expansion and equipping of an additional farm at
Malumfashi;
-    expanding training facilities at Daudawa and constructing
a new project training center at Malumfashi;
-    provision of adequate farmer extension, credit and market-
ing services, including staff, vehicles and equipment;
-    provision of seasonal and medium-term loans to farmers for
the purchase of crop inputs and farm equipment;
-    establishing an evaluation unit to evaluate the project; and
-    preparation of plans for the continued provision of project
services after the investment phase of the project is com-
pleted.
4.02      The project would be managed by the Funtua Project Management Unit
(FPMU) which would carry out the day-to-day management operations. FPMUT
would be responsible to the Funtua Project Executive Committee (FPEC) for
budgetary and policy control. A committee would be set up to coordinate
project activities at Federal level. Details at Chapter VI.



-7-
B. Detailed Features
4.03      A summary showing the expected scheduling of project activities
is at Annex 2, Table 1; it includes the construction program, the rate of
village involvement, and the number of farmers affected by the project
annually.
4.04      Agricultural Road Development.  The project would provide a network
of 1,500 km of agricultural roads, of a design adequate to allow light traf-
fic during most of the wet season, and heavy trucks during the dry season.
The roads would permit the efficient evacuation of farm produce, the timely
supply of inputs, the effective movement of project service staff, and much
improved general access to farms and hamlets. This basic infrastructure
would allow other Government programs including health and education to
expand into areas which currently are inaccessible. Annex 4 gives details
of construction methods, specifications, and plant and equipment require-
ments. In view of an acute shortage of local contracting capacity, and the
probable lack of interest of foreign contractors in work of this sort, con-
struction would be undertaken by force account through a project operated
and managed field construction unit which would make some use of petty con-
tractors and would thereby assist in their development. Maintenance of roads
and dams would be the responsibility of the Katsina Local Authority which has
a Workcs Department. In order to build up KLA operational effectiveness, KLA
road maintenance staff would be seconded to the project for training and oper-
ational control. The project would provide, during the investment period,
funds for the purchase and operation of the necessary maintenance equipment.
4.05      W4ater Development.  The project would set up and operate a Unit to
constru-ct soee 85 small and medium size earth dams, each of a minimum capacity
of 100,000 m , equipped with simple systems to reticulate the water away from
the reservoir.  The reservoirs would supply water for human and livestock
needs and would be located in strategic areas. They would also be used to
suDply water for compaction purposes during road construction. In addi-
tion, some 160 tapkis (ponds) would be developed to provide a better distribu-
tion of water for livestock for all but the last 3 months of the dry season.
In a very few instances, the dams and tapkis, where land and water availability
allows, would be used for the irrigation of village vegetable production.
Abundant dam sites are available. Annex 5 provides detail on design and
specifications.
4.06      Soil Conservation.  In association with the water development
program, soil conservation measure! would be undertaken within the catclh-
ment areas of the dams (about 5 km  each).  These would include the construc-
tion of cutoff ditches, artificial waterways and contour ridging and be
aimed to reduce siltation of the dams and control erosion. Further soil
conservation measures would be taken in providing the farmers with marker
ridges to assist in contour realignment of crop ridges. During the project
investment period, some 2,700 km or 36% of the project area would be so
protected; these programs should be continued beyond the investment period.
Annex 5 provides the details of this project component.



- 8 -
4.07      Building Development.  The project would include construction of
houses, offices and stores needed for project services. It would include
the construction of 350 houses of various sizes, a project office, 5 devel-
opment center offices, a railhead store and other facilities. In addition,
77 farm service centers, each including a 500-ton capacity warehouse would
be constructed, and improvements made to three market depots associated with
existing and future cotton ginneries. Those houses and buildings not re
quired by MANR after the project investment period would be taken over by
other Government departments operating within the area.
4.08      Seed Multiplication Farms.  The project would make improvements
to the existing seed multiplication farm at Daudawa; and expand and develop
the Malumfashi seed farm. Investments would include farm buildings, water
supplies, farm machinery and equipment. These seed farms would, in asso-
ciation with nearby large farmers, provide project and other State farmers
with improved seeds, act as demonstration and research centers, and provide
practical training in farm management. Details are at Annex 3, Schedule 1.
During loan negotiations, assurances were obtained from NCS that not later
than one month from loan effective date management responsibilities for Daudawa
and tfalumfashi seed farms, together with their assets and staff, would be
under the care of FPMUT.
4.09      Training Facilities.  The project would expand boarding accommo-
dation at the Daudawa training center from 20 to 40 trainees and construct
a new center at Malumfashi, also catering for 40 residential trainees.
Initially, the centers would be used for project staff training, but pro-
gressively emphasis would be on farmer training.
4.10      Farmer Support Services.  The project would expand and intensify
support services for farmers. It would increase the farmer extension persort-
nel ratio from the current 1:2,440 to 1:240. This would involve an increase
in extension agents from 41 to 420. The extension personnel would include,
besides basic extension agents, personnel specialized in livestock husbandry,
seed multiplication, commercial farm management, farmer training, and farm
equipment. The tractor hire service now operated by MANR at a loss would
b    .¶scc out, and gradually replaced by project sponsored and assisted private
operators. A credit service would be established to operate a project con-
trolled credit revolving fund and the contingent services to farmers (para
6.1q).  A marketing service would also be established to improve market
liaison, quality control, price information, produce weighing and to control
traders. The credit and marketing services would employ some 260 staff of
various grades; of these only about 30, mainly produce examiners are now
employed by MNR. Consequently, the majority of personnel employed in these
services would receive in-service training from the project (details are at
Annex 6).
4.11      Other MANP, services such as veterinary, fisheries and forestry would
continue to worlc within the project area, and would coordinate their
activities with FPM.



- 9 --
4.12      Farm Inputs.  Under the project, seasonal inputs would be made
available to farmers for cash and credit.  Such inputs and equipment are
estimated to amount, during the project investment period, to 56,000 tons
of fertilizer, 4,000 tons improved seeds, 2,000 tons insecticides, 47,000
ultra low volume insecticide sprayers, 10,000 ox carts, 10,000 ox plows and
100 tractors and associated implements. Inputs and equipment would be sold
for cash or credit at the farm service centers. Details are at Annex 7 and 8.
4.13      Project MLonitoring and Evaluation.  A Project Monitoring and Evalua-
tion Unit (PMEU) would be established by the Federal Ministry of Agriculture
and Natural Resources (FMANR). PNEU would comprise two sections, the first
would technically review and oversee financial records and statements in
respect of the project, and the second would develop a system of evaluation
and analysis to provide EIG, State Governments and other institutions, relevant
economic and social data on the success or failure of project policies. Such
information would be vital to long term agricultural and rural planning in
Nigeria. FMG has indicated that it would employ consultants to staff the
second section.  PMEU would give technical direction to FPMU administered field
evaluation units to ensure adequate collection of information for PMEU analysis.
During loan negotiations assurances were obtained that arrangements accept-
able to the Bank would be drawn up between FMIG and consultants acceptable to
the Bank setting out the terms of reference for the evaluation section of
PNEU and the services to be provided by the consultants. The latter would be
one of the Nigerian universities, probably the Ahmadu Bello University. Fur-
ther details are at Annex 9.
4.14      Provision would be made for the necessary support items such as
motor vehicles, equipment, etc. to ensure that the objectives of the project
are fulfilled. Details at Annex 10.
4.15      Post Project Development.  During the project investment period,
TLANR and FPT4T would draw up detailed plans and arrange for the allocation
of staff to allow project services to continue undisrupted to farmers within
the project area after the end of the investment period. Presupposing no
radical changes in NCS organization extension and development services would
be handled by MANR's regular provincial organization suitably strengthened;
the evaluation section of PME.U would probably continue to evaluate this and
other projects, and the field evaluation units would be absorbed by the MANR
Planning Unit; NSMB would take over market control and liaison; ans4 the
Katsina Iocal Authority (KLA) or any other appropriate agency would maintain
roads and water supplies provided under the project. Assurances that roads
and dams would be maintained were obtained during negotiations.
4.16      During the project the evaluation section of PMEU would consider
various alternatives for the future use and operations of the revolving credit
fund,and the supply of farm inputs. It is important that by 1980 credit and
farm supplies should be commercially orientated, and that the institutions
involved should be relatively independent of Government with control in the
hands of participating farmers. Prior to negotiations the intention was to
set up a Farmer Finance Company to control and operate the revolving credit



- 10 -
fund. During negotiations FMG indicated that it would prefer to postpone the
formation of such a company to a time when (a) further evaluations of farmer
credit institutions and requirements had been made by PMEU during the first
three years of the project; and (b) evidence, if any, of improvements in the
performance of the existing cooperative system under new Federal policy and
guidance. The exclusion of the proposed Farmer Finance Company should have no
edverse effect on the project during the development period, provided that
firm plans are made for the proper transfer of the fund to the appropriate
institution prior to the project's completion. Thus, PMEU would play an
important role in evaluating the problems and requirements for future commer-
cial services to project farmers.

5.01      Project costs are estimated to total N37.9 million (US$57.6 mil-
lion) of which the foreign exchange component would be N19.1 million (US$29.2
million) or 51% of total costs.

5.03      Project costs include a 5% physical contingency, and a variable
price contingency 1/ over the proposed five-year loan disbursement period.
Contingencies amount to N9.1 million (US$13.8 million) or 24% of total project
costs. Costs are based on current quotations for vehicles, plant, equipment,

were amended at negotiations to reflect current prices, and because the
average current 1980 prices liave been projected to be about the same as
current 1974 prices, price contingencies for fertilizer have not been
included.

materials and farm inputs; on current contract rates for building construction;
and on up-to-date salary and wage scales inclusive of allowances. Costs of
expatriate staff are based on current international salaries, and include
standard benefits and allowances. In the case of farm inputs, only incremental
requirements are costed. Participating farmers would, as now, receive a 50%
subsidy on the cost of phosphatic and nitrogenous fertilizers and the costs
of this subsidy is included in project costs. Project costs include import
duties and taxes (internationally recruited staff salaries are estimated net
of tax). Total taxes amount to N2.5 million (US$3.8 million). Analysis of
the costs, Annex 10, Table 1, shows that 6% (N2.1 million) is for project
administration; 24% (N9.1 million) is for extension, credit and market serv-
ices; 12% (N4.7 million) is for road, soil and water conservation; 30% (N11.4
million) for incremental farm inputs and fertilizer subsidies; 4% (N1.5 million)
for project evaluation; and 30% (N9.1 million) for contingencies. Internation-
ally recruited staff costs are estimated at 3% of total costs, about half of
which would be for specialized technicians currently unavailable or in cri-
tically short supply in Nigeria.
B. Proposed Financing
5.04      It is proposed that IBRD make a loan of US$29 million to FMG, re-
payable at 8% over 20 years including a five-year grace period for principal
during which interest and commitment charges on the loan would be paid.
Equivalent to 51% of total project costs the loan would cover all foreign
costs, estimated at US$29 million (N19.1 million). Of the US$29 million,
N18.4 million (US$27.9 million) would be onlent to the NCS. The remaining
NO.7 rillion (US$1.1 million) would be retained by FMG to finance the eval-
uation section of the Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU). During
negotiations it was agreed that the subsidiary loan agreement between FMG
and NCS would have been approved by the Bank.
5.05      FMG would provide NO.4 million (US$0.7 million) or 1% of total
project cost as its contribution to the evaluation section of PMEU. NCS
wrtild provide N16.4 million (US$25 million) or 43% of total project cost as
its contribution to investment and operating costs. The remainder of project
costs estimated at N1.9 million (US$2.9 million) or 5% would be contributed
by participating farmers in the form of cash purchases of seasonal inputs and
down payments on farm equipment. Details of the project financing plan are
shown at Annex 11, Table 1, and are summarized in the following table:

C. Procurement
5.07      Procurement of vehicles, plant, equipment, fertilizers, insecticides,
farm tractors and implements, where orders or contracts had a value of more
than IUS$25,000, would be through international competitive bidding (ICB) in
accordance with IBRD guidelines. Such procurement is estimated to have a
value of about N13.5 million (US$20.6 million). Domestically manufactured
goods would be allowed a 15% preference when comparing domestic bids with
those of foreign manufacturers. Contracts for the construction of buildings,
houses, and the purchase of construction materials and furnishings valued
at R5.0 million (US$7.5 million) would not be attractive to foreign contractors
due to the dispersed location of the work and small size of individual contracts
and orders, and would be awarded under locally advertised competitive bidding.
In the latter case, foreign firms and suppliers would not be precluded from
bidding as such work would be advertized in at least one local newspaper, but
bonafide domestic contractors would receive a 7-1/2% preference in bid evalua-
tion. Construction of roads, water supplies and soil conservation works esti-
mated at N4.7 million (US$7.1 million) would be carried out on force account
due to the shortage of suitable medium scale contractors who normally would
undertak'e this work, however petty contractors would be employed to the max-
ir'un extent feasible.  A large part of project costs, an estimated 110.2 mil-
lion (!S$15.5 million), would be for labor, staff salaries and allowances, ve-
hicle maintenance and operation, general services, and project evaluation, and
as such generally be unsuitable for competitive bidding. Those items not in-
cluded specifically under procedures for ICB or local competitive bidding would
be purchased through local commercial channels. The remaining N9.1 million
(US$13.9 million) of nroject costs would be contingencies. Nigeria has no
preferential import tariff arrangements and competition between foreign manu-
facturers on the domestic marlcet is keen.
D. Disbursement
5.08      The nroceeds of the Bank loan (US$29 million) would be disbursed
over five years (1975/76-1979/80) to cover 51% of total project costs and
would be expended against the following categories:
-    100% of the cif cost of directly imported vehicles,
heavy plant, and equipment (Annex 10, Table 2) or 75%
of the cost of these items if locally procured totalling
US$2.4 million (M1.6 million):
-    1007 of the cif cost of directly imnorted farm tractors
and equipment, ox drawn equipment, spraying machines,
fertilizers, insecticides, battery sets and seeds
(Annex 7, Tables 2-5) or 72% of the cost of these
items if procured locally totalling US$6.7 million
(N4.4 million);



- 15 -
-    100% of the cost of internationally recruited staff
(Annex 10, Table 4) totalling US$1.8 million (N1.2
million);
-    60% of the cost of buildings, houses, furnishings,
and construction material and labor for roads, dams
and soil conservation works (Annex 10, Table 3) total-
ling US$4.5 million (N3.0 million);
-    60% of local staff costs (Annex 10, Table 4) totalling
US$3.7 million (N2.4 million);
-    60% of the cost of the evaluation section of the Pro-
ject Monitoring and Evaluation Unit and field evalua-
tion units (Annex 9, Table 1) US$1.4 million (NO.9
million); and
-    an unallocated amount totalling US$8.5 million
(N5.6 million) to cover physical and price contingen-
cies.
5.09      Disbursements of the Bank loan would be against import documentation,
contracts and certified records of expenditures. For disbursements made
against certified records of expenditures, documentation would not be sub-
mitted for review as a matter of course, but would be retained by the proj-
ect management unit for scrutiny by Bank supervision missions. Any surplus
loan funds after the completion of the project would be used to further
develop the project area providing expanded services to farmers.
E. Budgetary Control Funding Procedures Accounts and Audit
5.10      Budgets.  Annual budgets would be prepared by FPMIJ and approved
by FPEC for inclusion in the MANR development budget. The initial budget
would use as its base the cost estimates set out in this report, but would
be amended where necessary to tie in with changes in costs and project poli-
cies and development schedules. Major changes in policies and procedures
have to be approved by FPEC and the Bank. FPMU would, thereafter, amend its
monthly cash flow statement indicating costs, revenues and working capital
requirements. These monthly statements would be submitted to FPEC for mon-
itoring but FPNU would be authorized to operate on such monthly statements
unless officially informed to the contrary by the Chairman of FPEC. Assur-
ances that the above arrangements would be employed were obtained from NCS
during negotiations.
5.11      Funding Procedures.  On the basis of the annual budget approved by
FPEC, NCS would make the necessary allocations in its annual development
budget to cover its contribution to project costs as well as about 25% of



- 16 -
the remaining annual costs of the project to meet working capital require-
ments, the latter amounting to about Nl million. In order to insure an ade-
quate flow of funds to the Project, assurances were obtained during negotia-
tions, that when necessary adequate guaranteed draw down procedures would be
made by NCS with a bank of its choice to allow FPMU full access to funds
within the limitation of the approved annual budget. As a condition of loan
effectiveness, NCS would furnish the Bank with satisfactory evidence that a
project bank account with adequate funds had been established.
5.12      Accounts and Audit.  Separate accounts for FPMU and the evaluation
section of PMEU would be kept under acceptable commercial accounting proce-
dures. However, such accounts would be set up to facilitate the retrieval of
cost accounting and project evaluation data, as well as for audit control.
All the accounts would be audited annually by independent auditors, mutually
acceptable to NCS, FMG and the Bank. Audited annual accounts, balance sheets
and operating statements for FPMU and PMEU would be submitted simultaneously
to NCS, FMG and the Bank within four months of the closing of the financial
year. During negotiations, assurances to these effects were obtained.
VI. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Organization
6.01      The project being the first of its kind in the State would have
to be carefully controlled. Because of its size and responsibilities for
various development aspects not normally associated with any one Government
Ministry, it would need a degree of autonomy    not normally given to Ministerial
Departments. Thus, the project would be implemented by the Funtua Project
Management Unit (FPMU), headed by a Project Manager who would be responsibile
to the Funtua Project Executive Committee (FPEC); the chairman of which would
be the Permanent Secretary of the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources
of NCS. FPMU would have its headquarters at Funtua, the Divisional town and
center of the project area. FPMU would coordinate its activities with other
similar projects at a Federal level through a Federal Coordinating Committee.
As a condition of loan effectiveness, the North Central State Executive Council
would gazette an order, mutually acceptable to FMG and the Bank, establishing
FPMU, setting out its terms of reference and responsibilities. Assurances
to this effect were obtained during negotiations (a draft proposal for the
gazetted order is at Annex 6, Schedule 2).
6.02      Funtua Project Executive Committee would, inter alia, determine
policy, coordinate project activities with those of associated State Ministries
and Departments, exercise buidgetary and audit control, approve the appoint-
ment of professional grade staff, and review and set salary scales. FPEC
would meet at least once a quarter and would comprise: the Permanent Secretary
MANR as Chairman, and representatives of the Ministries of Finance, Local Gov-
erment, Works, Economic Planning, and NCS Water Board, a representative of
the Federal Department of Agriculture, Chief Agricultural Officer for the



- 17 -
North Central State, and two members residing in the project area to be
appointed by the Military Governor. The Project Manager would be Secretary
of FPEC.
6.03      A Federal Coordinating Committee would be established to review the
progress of the Funtua and similar projects in the northern States. It would
provide coordination between other Bank-financed agricultural projects of sim-
ilar nature in northern Nigeria, and would insure a full exchange of ideas
and results. In its review of project progress, it would be aided by the
evaluation section of the Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU). The
committee would meet at least twice a year and would comprise: The Director
of the Federal Department of Agricultural (FDA) as Chairman; Chief Agricul-
tural Planning Officer (FDA) as Secretary; a representative from the respective
project executive committees; Project Managers of Funtua, Gombe and Gusau;
Director of the Institute of Agricultural Research, the Head of the Evaluation
Section of PMEU, representatives of NAB and NPMC; and representatives of the
Federal Ministry of Finance. During negotiations assurances were obtained
that a Federal Coordinating Committee would be established, with terms of
reference and composition satisfactory to the Bank within 3 months of loan
effectiveness.
6.04      Project Administration would fall under the direct responsibility
of the Project Manager for all operational procedures and actions. He would
be responsible to FPEC. The FPEC, in consultation with the Project Manager,
would appoint one of the senior project staff as Deputy Project MIanager. The
Project Manager would be assisted by: Chief Administrative Officer, Chief
Accountant, Chief Technical Officer, Chief Engineer, Chief Marketing Officer,
and Credit Manager (Annex 6, Schedule 3 provides details of the tasks of key
personnel). A summary showing the functions of the project's six operational
sections follows:
(a) Administration Section would be responsible for all project person-
nel matters, staff transportation, registry, security and public
relations.
(b) Finance Section would be responsible for project accounts, pro-
curement, stores, internal audit, budget control and would set
up systems to provide accurate cost accounts for evaluation and
management purposes.
(c) Technical Services Section would be responsible for farm extension
advice, staff and farmer training, seed multiplication, farm
management advice to larger farmers, and tractor hire services.
The main development effort would be through the extension service
and structurally it would consist of five development unit centers
based on the District towns of Funtua, Malumfashi, Faskari,
Kankara and Bakori, each headed by a Development officer, 1/
1/   Normally the minimum grade of the officer filling this post would be that
of agricultural officer. Hlowever, in the early stages of the project, it
is likely that most of the posts would be filled by agricultural superin-
tendents.



- 18 -
who would supervise agricultural assistants (AA) posted to farm
service centers in each of the 77 villages of the project area.
Each AA would supervise an average of four agricultural instructors
(Al) who would live in the hamlets associated with each village
and who would work with the hamlet farmers and their committees.
In this way, the extension system would be tailored to the trade-
itional village structure.
(d)  Credit Services Section.  Credit services would work parallel with
the extension services. Credit Officers would be posted to Devel-
opment Unit Centers, and two Credit Assistants to each farm
service center.
(e)  Market Services Section would monitor the existing marketing system
liaise and provide market information for buyers and farmers, con-
trol the activities of Licensed Buying Agents (LBA), and supervise
the work of produce examiners (PE). A Marketing Supervisor would
be assisted by two Marketing Assistants posted to each Development
Unit Center and a Produce Examiner would be posted to each market.
Market Services would be responsible for the logistics involved in
procurement of farm inputs and for their distribution and handling.
As such, the produce examiner would carry out the functions of
store keeper when the markets are closed.
(f) Engineering Services Section would be a specialized service
responsible for land use planning, road construction, water devel-
opment, soil conservation, building supervision and workshop
management. It would prepare tender documentation for procure-
ment of engineering equipment and supplies, supervise building
contractors, and implement all construction aspects of the project
not undertaken by contractors. It would maintain project vehicles
and equipment.
6.05      Field evaluation would be directed by the evaluation section of the
Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU).  The field enumeration staff
would be administratively responsible to the Project Manager. Details of the
field unit are at Annex 9.
B. Staffing
6.06      Both Federal and State Governments recognize the need for qualified
and experienced staff to administer the project and assure high technical
standards in its field implementation. The northern States are generally
short of qualified personnel to fill key project management and senior
technical posts, and the demand for personnel with such expertise is high.
NCS is fully aware of the problem of staffing the project adequately and
is prepared to recruit staff internationally where qualified Nigerian personnel



- 19 -
are not available. Medium level technical and administrative staff needed
for the project are generally available in Nigeria, though not necessarily
in the North Central State. Most lower level staff needs would be met from
within the State: existing civil servants; or the output of existing training
schemes; or staff recruited and trained directly by the project.
6.07      Management Staff.  International recruitment is expected to be
necessary for the Project Manager, Chief Accountant, Credit Manager, Chief
Engineer, and Chief Technical Officer. The Chief Admnistrative Officer and
Chief Marketing Officer would be Nigerian appointments. During negotiations
assurances were obtained that the qualifications, experience and terms and
conditions of employment of appointees to the above posts would be mutually
acceptable to the Bank and to NCS. A condition of effectiveness would be
that the Project Manager, Chief Administrative Officer, Chief Accountant,
Chief Engineer and Chief Technical Officer had been appointed.
6.08      High Level Technical Staff.  Nigeria is critically short of experi-
enced technical personnel. Given the shortage of experienced and available
technical staff it is likely that two farm managers, a seeds officer, an
animal husbandry officer, a farm management advisor, a land use planner, a
road engineer, a mechanical engineer, a water and soil conservation engineer
and a senior evaluation officer would have to be recruited internationally.
During negotiations assurances were obtained that their qualifications and
terms and conditions of employment would be acceptable to the Bank and NCS.
6.09      Medium Level Staff.  Professional officer (eg Agricultural Officer)
or senior technical officer (eg Agricultural Superintendent) grades would be
recruited from within the State or from other States in Nigeria. All field
technical, credit and marketing service staff would be required to be Hausa
speaking. Miedium and lower level staff exist, although many are assigned
currently to non productive posts.   During negotiations assurances were ob-
tained from NCS that at least 2 Agricultural Officers, 13 agricultural super-
intendents and 51 agricultural assistants or experienced agricultural instructors
would be made available to the project during the first two years.
6.10     tWhere possible project staff, particularly agricultural staff,
would be appointed to the project as Government employees on full pensionable
service. Expatriate staff and other Nigerian staff such as engineers, credit
and marketing personnel would be appointed on three year contracts renewable
on negotiation. Salaries would generally follow acceptable Government scales.
Every effort would be made to avoid transfer of staff away from the project,
except on the grounds of unsuitability, and assurances were obtained during
negotiations that transfers of senior staff into and from the project organi-
zation would be undertaken only after consultation with FPEC.
6.11      Project staff of technical grades (or equivalent) and above would be
provided with project housing both at Funtua and in the field. Non technical
grades such as agricultural instructors (Al) and produce examiners (PE), as
well as clerks, drivers, messengers etc. would be expected to find their own



- 20 -
accommodation in the towns and villages. At the discretion of project manage-
ment, staff would be provided with project transport. Project staff would
qualify for normal Government allowances. Annex 10 tables 3-4 gives details.
Due to expected delays in construction of houses, costs are included for
renting private accommodation (Annex 10, Table 6).
6.12      Annex 9 details staff requirements for the evaluation section of
PMEU. During negotiations assurances were obtained that senior appointments
to the evaluation section of PMEU would be suitably qualified and appointed
on terms and conditions acceptable to FMG and the Bank.
C. Staff Training
6.13      FPMU would undertake the training of lower level staff through
special courses at project training centers (para 4.09). The trainlng. of
higher level staff would be undertaken on an inservice basis. FPMU woudd pay
particular attention to training selected staff to fill managerial positions
as these are vacated by internationally recruited personnnel (see Annex 6,
Table 1-3 for possible schedule) and would undertake as requested to train
staff to be employed for other NCS development projects. Such staff would
receive practical management training on the project and would have the
opportunity of decision making at various levels within the project, but
would retain executive responsibility for their own section of the project.
NCS and the Bank would ensure that adequate training of potential manage-
ment staff was undertaken, and FPMU management would report regularly on the
progress of project training.
D.  Farm Inputs - Procurement and Distribution and Credit Procedur.4s
6.14      The project would be responsible for the procurement, distribution
and sale, for cash or credit, of fertilizers, seeds, insecticides, sprayers,
oxdrawn equipment, tractors, and other necessary farm supplies; and would
decide on the suitability and composition of any package of inputs offered
to farmers. It is expected that 75% of seasonal inputs and 100% of farm
equipment would be sold on credit. The value of inputs sold during the
project life is estimated at N10.6 million for fertilizer; N4.5 million for
insecticides, seeds, and batteries; and N 3.0 million for farm equipment;
a total of N18.1 million. The net cash requirement for inputs would be
N11.4 million of which N5.3 million would be the NCS subsidy on fertilizers.
Annex 7 describes farm inputs and their procurement.
6.15      Project marketing services would be responsible, on advice of the
technical staff, for the procurement of farm inputs and for the logistics
involved in their handling within the project area.



- 21 -
6.16      Currentlv NCS subsidises nitrogenous and phosphatic fertilizers
bv between 30 and 50% of their cost delivered MANR store. The actual rate
depends on the efficiency and cost of NCS handling. Under the project
farmers would receive nitrogenous and phosnhatic fertilizer at 50% of cost
cif farm service center. Such a subsidy is justified (a) as an incentive
for farmers to use fertilizer, 1/ and (b) to compensate for generally low
marketing board prices offered for statutory marketed crops. During loan
negotiations, assurances were obtained that the present subsidy on fertilizers
would be maintained. Currently insecticides receive a 50% subsidy. However
as usage is insignificant at present and the benefits of their use clearly
discernible and profitable insecticides would be sold to farmers at their
full cost.
Farm Credit
6.17      The organization of the delivery and recovery of farm credit is
described in Annex 8. The net incremental credit requirement and gross credit
disbursed during the five year project period is estimated at N4.7 million
and N9.8 million respectively.
6.18      FPMU would set up a revolving credit fund to be operated by the
Credit Services Section of the project. Prior to the beginning of each plant-
ing season, a schedule of proposed lending terms would be furnished by FPMU
to FPEC, which in consultation with appropriate Nigerian authorities and the
Bank would apply the lending terms for the said season. Interest rates for
sub loans would be between 5-9 1/2%, and would be determined on a basis of
the category of farmers or type of inputs to be provided. Assurances to
these effects were obtained during negotiations.
6.19      As a basis for preparing crop budgets shown at Annex 1, it is assumed
that FPMU would lend at a flat service rate of 10%, a charge that can be
afforded by the farmers and would include sufficient margin to cover bad debts
etc. Repayments would be made for seasonal loans within 12 months; sprayers
over 2 years; ox drawn equipment over 3 years; and tractors and implements
over 4 years. Except in cases of crop failure, when a moratorium would be
declared by FPEC, a borrower not repaying his loan by the due date would be
charged a penalty of 1% per month. Funds have not been specifically provided
under the loan for cash credits to cover the employment of farm labor; as it
is considered that any additional labor requirements would be met from within
the Family unit, particularly as the project is directed towards the small
family operated farm.  Hlowever, in the light of current changes in Nigeria,
specifically the advent of compulsory free education, the need for hired
labor may increase. During the project life FPMJ in association with PMEU
would establish the parameters, if any, for cash credits, and the logistics
involved in their disbursement and collection. If the findings are positive,
the Government and the Bank would agree on the utilization of local funds or
disbursements from undisbursed portions of the Bank loan.
1/   The gross cost of fertilizer is high in northern Nigeria, and to induce
the farmer to use more fertilizer subsidies are an added incentive.
Fertilizer subsidies are general throughout Nigeria.



- 22 -
6.20      The revolving credit fund is an intimate part of the project, and
of necessity has to evolve into a more permanent institution so that farmers
can utilize its funds after the project is completec7.  Such an institution
rihlit take t'he form of cooperatives, farmer finance companies, or branches
of state or national credit banks.  So as to ensure a proper transfer of re-
sponsibilities, the Fvaluation Section of PIMEX would, as one of its duties,
determine the future institutional form of the fund, taking into account the
performance of existing lending institutions and the needs of the farmers.
6.21      Operating procedures, which are described in Annex 8 would be
subject to modification as necessary, would be devised by the project credit
service but would include the following essential components: lending would
be limited to input items carried by the project at its service center stores,
and be in amounts specified on a simple farm management plan drawn up for
each borrower by extension service staff. Sale of items would be recorded
on a bookkeeping machine. As the farm service centers develop into the main
markets for cash crops, farmers would be encouraged to repay their debts to
FPMU at time of sale.  All borrowers would lhave a laminated identity card
containing their photograph, serial number, and signature or mark to
facilitate identification and record keeping.
6.22      The creditworthiness of each loan applicant would be certified by
lis hamlet or village head, or a project approved farmers association; this
would be part of the mutual guarantee policy that FPMU would pursue. Under
this, once a year FPM' would review all hamlet accounts, and where defaults
were found the village, hamlet or farmer association authorities, whichever
the smallest unit, would be informed of their defaulters and urged to collect
from defaulters, unless due cause is presented for default. If defaults
were not made good, FPM would withhold credit to all farmers in the default-
ing unit.  It is believed that this teclnique would be a powerful influence
in avoiding default, under northern Nigerian circumstances. Provision would
b)e made to extend credit to groups or to village farmer associations if
farmers wish to pool the input requirements and assume joint liability for
the credit.
VII. PRODUCTION, .MARKEMTS, FARMERS' BEN4EFITS AND
FINANCIAL IMPLICATION TO GOVERNMENT
A. Yields and Outputs
7.01      The complexity of cropping mixtures and patterns in the project
area make yield variations between farms inevitable and substantial. It has
been assumed, however, that by project maturity in 1980 project area farmers
would fall into one of two or three categories for each of the five crops
that would be influenced by the prcject. The categories would be determined



- 23 -
by the extent to which the farmer had taken up improved practices.  Details
are given in Annex 1.  Yield improvements and increases in gross production
due to project actions are summarized for eacli of the five crops:

7.02      Yield improvements are conservative and fall well within experimental
and field results obtained in northern Nigeria and other similar ecological
zones elsewhere in West Africa. The proposed crop inputs to achieve these
expected increase hlave been well tested, and are economic. Further details
are at Annexes 1 and 3.
B.  Markets and Prices
7.013      Currently food crops grown in the project area are marketed via
rural r,arkets.  Mfarketing is not efficient since although local competition
mav 'De strong, the market open to the farmer is very small and only a fraction
of the national market. The main defects spring from the fragmented nature
of the marIket and farmers' lack of both accurate information and bargaining
power; the project would seek to rectify this position (details are given in
Annex 12, paras 38 and 39).
7.04       Following an FMG announcement in early 1973 statutory marketing of
cotton and groundnuts together with other cashl crops in Nigeria is undergoing
a series of organizational chIanges whichi include: (a) federal responsibility
for producer price determination and support price policies; this could lead
eventuallv to subsidization of primary and secondary marketing operations by
FMG; (b) albolition of producer taxes; (c) closer relationslhip between world
and producer prices and thlus an end to indirect taxation through accumulation
of surpluses by the IMarketing Boards; and (d) the Northiern States Marketing
Board (NRS,5B) becoming an agent for the N4igerian Produice ilarketing Company
(;NfC), the latter wholly oined by FMG now being the price controlling and
setting organization- within the statutory system.  Thle reforms are being
effected and the 1974 producer prices reflect the actual world market situa-
tion, and should provide considerable production incentives.
7.05      As thiere is need for considerable improvement in primary marketing
the project would take various measures to improve marketing in general. !Jore
specifically, the project would provide improved weighing and measuring devices



- 24 -
an(d village storage facilities, and would relocate markets to improve the ease
of crop marketing. For the statutory crops a number of marketing reforms would
be necessary, such reforms initially to be confined to the project areas in-
clude: (a) timely announcement of producer prices; (b) provision of adequate
funds to LBA for produce purchase; 1/ (c) establishment and enforcement of
quality standards; and (d) disciplining of inefficient or dishonest LBA and
produce inspection staff.
7.06      During negotiations assurances were obtained that:
(i) NCS would cause the NSMB to pay particular attention
to efficient produce buying within the project area;
and
(ii)  where necessary encourage groups of farmers to operate
as Licensed Buying Agents within the Project Area.
7.07      Producer prices for industrial and export crops, such as cotton and
g,roundnuts, are expected under the new PFG policies to improve during the
early years of the project, but would then be expected to decline by 1980 in
line with prices projected by the Bank's Economic Analysis and Projections
Department. Food crop prices are expected to decline somewhat from present
levels in consequence of more intensive cereal production in the project area
buLt are expected to remain substantially above world (export parity) prices.

C. Farmer Benefits
7.08      Farmers participating in project activities would obtain substantial
direct income benefits through utilizing project services and inputs. Addi-
tional indirect benefits due to improved roads, water supplies, soil conserva-
tion and marketing would accrue to all farmers within the project area.

the overall benefits of improved technology over traditional practices, and

7.10      The report has made no attempt to produce a typical farm budget
since farm sizes and production combinations vary considerably throughout
the project area, and any such budget would not be realistic. Instead,
expected farmer benefits have been derived from a series of crop budgets for
1 ha units of production. Project actions would result in measurable addi-
tions to net family incomes totalling N9.7 million per annum in 1980. Stud-
ies undertaken in areas similar to the project show net farm incomes includ-
ing income from livestock and the value of produce consumed to be in the order
of N175 (US$266) per family or about N27 (US$41) per capita. The net income
for the five project crops accounts for about N143 (US$217) per family. The
additional net income derived from the improved output of the five crops
would be N96 (US$146) per family, an increase of 67%. The average per capita
income would increase from US$41 to US$63. Such an improvement infers a
carowth in net farm income of 8% per annum and would appear feasible given
efficient project management.
D. Financial Implication for Government
7.11      The cash flow for NCS is summarized in Annex 11, Table 2.  The
average cost to NCS inclusive of loan servicing, would be about M5.5 million
annually until 1995. Thereafter the cost would be reduced to N3.4 million
annually. Considerable reductions, in Government support finance, could be
made if and when the fertilizer subsidy is reduced or totally removed; such
savings would an-ount to N2.1 million annually. NCS could further recoup
up to N4.7 million when the revolving credit fund is institutionalized, there-
by allowing NCS to retire part or all of its capital. The recent abolition
of produce taxes on statutory crops throughout Nigeria, including cotton
and grondnuts, would result in NCS receiving no tangible financial benefits
from the project.
7.12      The Federal Government would, through secondary or teritiary mul-
tiplier benefits, conservatively estimated for Nigeria at between 8 to 20%,
collect indirect taxation revenues of about N 1.5 million annually.
VIII.  ECONOTaC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
8.01      The project's principal direct benefit would be the incremental
increase in farm production that it would generate, and which would result
directly in higher incomes for some 60,000 farm families, and better living
standards for a further 40,000 who would benefit through the improved
infrastructure and marketing arrangements within the project area. If the
non-farming population, traders, laborers, etc. are included, then about
900,000 people would in one way or other receive primary or secondary benefits
througl project activities.  The economic rate of return from investment in
the project is estimated at 41%.  Important assumntions used in the calcula-
tion at Annex 13 are:



- 27 -
a.   a shadow wage rate of N 0.40 per 6 hr man/day,
as against the official rate of N 0.60;
b.   the exclusion of identifiable taxes from economic costs;
c.   a shadow foreign exchange rate of US$1.27 per N 1.00 as
against the official rate of US$1.52 per N 1.00;
d.   economic farm gate prices for 1980 in 1974 constant Naira
as projected by the Bank's Economic Analysis and Projections
Division (para 7.07) based on export parity for groundnuts
and cotton and import substitution for maize, sorghum and
cowpeas; and
e.   the exclusion of water and associated soil conservation
development costs, and project evaluation costs, since
the benefits attributed to them are intangible.
8.02      If no ajustment is made for the shadow rate of foreign exchange,
the economic rate of return would be reduced to 32%. It is possible that the
project might not meet the proposed production targets for a number of reasons,
including poor management, lack of farmer response, and the possible failure
of delivery of farm inputs due to uncontrollable circumstances; and as such
it is important to test the economic rate of return against various levels of
reduced production and the associated reduction in farm inputs, allowing all
other factors to remain constant. This analysis, detailed in Annex 13 is
summarized below:
Production levels as percentage of estimated
Project Output

AFE /2      32      28       25      21      17      12
/1   Shadow Foreign Exchange rate
/2   Actual Foreign Exchange rate
3.03      The project would have important secondary benefits that would
accrue both to the people of the project area, and for the State and Federation
as a whole.  The more important ones would be for:
a.   the people of the project area would benefit from:  (a) a
general improvemcnt in communications and public utilities,
due to project actions initiating change and providing a



- 28 -
basis for extending, by other Government institutions,
social improvements including health and education; (b) an
expected increase in local trade that would offer better
opportunities of employment for the non-farming population;
and (c) the strengthening of local institutions including
the local authorities and local departments of Government.
b.   the North Central State would benefit principally through
part of its population being stabilized and becoming more
productive. Increased net employment accounting for 12,000
man years per annum would result from projected actions.
The State would be able to use the project to test new
development ideas that could, if successful, be easily
transposed together with trained staff to other areas.
The Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources would
be strengthened, and would derive from the project new
credit and marketing techniques that could be easily
applied to other State farmers. The project would set
up a seed multiplication center that would provide seed
to other farmers elsewhere in the State. Finally the pro-
ject would produce additional raw material and would demand
additional input resources to support the growth of the
State's industrial expansion.
C.   the Federal Republic of Nigeria would benefit from annual net
incremental foreign exchange earnings amounting at full pro-
duction to about N14 million (US$21 million). Project eval-
uation through the project established evaluation unit would
provide a wealth of information that could assist in deter-
mining Federal agricultural policy, and resource and finan-
cial allocations throughout Nigeria.
3.04      Even though the economic rate of return appears very favorable;
in the long run indirect tangible benefits and intangible benefits may prove
far more valuable than direct tangible benefits. Improved farm management
practices and profits to farming would far outlive the project. A step would
be taken in the right direction to reduce income disparity, and to ensure a
more even food supply for Nigeria's urban populations and expanding commercial
livestock industries.  Perhaps most importantly the people of the project
area would have the peace of mind that would come from more assured water
supplies in the dry season, the added confidence in an improved marketing
system, the satisfaction of a little more money to spend, and the hope that
they and their children would live a little better in the future.

project (para 4.15);

dent auditors, mutually acceptable to NCS and the Bank, and
