ANNEX 1
NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
Funtua Agricultural Development Project
Farm and Crop Development
A. General
1.        The major crops in the project area are sorghum and millet, cotton,
groundnuts and cowpeas. Minor crops include maize, sugarcane, chilies and
okra. Production data for cotton and groundnuts are compiled from purchases
by the Northern States Marketing Board (NSMB) (Tables 1 and 2). No pro-
duction data are available for other crops; nor is there other than qualitative
information on farmers' yields, farm sizes or cropping patterns. However,
a detailed economic survey of three villages within 60 km of the project area
has been carried out and published recently. 1/ Ecological and sociological
conditions are comparable in many respects between the two areas and, for this
reason, the survey has been used as a basis for the formulation of assumptions
on present and projected farming activities.
2.        Cattle, sheep and goats are kept throughout the project area by
farmers, and in substantially larger numbers by semi-nomadic pastoralists
(Fulani). The latter utilize fallow land (a rapidly diminishing resource),
forest and grazing reserves, and crop residues for their livestock. They
exist in an increasingly uneasy symbiosis with settled cultivators as pressure
on land increases with rising populations. Livestock management systems are
generally poor and productivity is low. There are no reliable data on stock
numbers.
3.        Cultivations are carried out mainly by hand hoe and cutlass.  How-
ever, some 7,000 farmers (7%) are reported to own work oxen and equipment.
In 1972/73 some 150 ha were ploughed and some 400 ha were ridged mechanically
by MANR tractor hiring services.
B. The Influence of Climate on Cropping
4.        From May to October, the widgst range between mean monthly maximum
and minimum temperatures is 100° to 70  F (38  C to 21° C).  At project area
altitudes of 600 to 900 m cold spells do not cause any problems and rainfall
is the major factor for limiting crop growth. Monthly and annual data for
2 stations are given at Tables 3 & 4. Instead of presenting these in the
1/   An Economic Survey of three villages in Zaria Province, Samaru
Miscellaneous Paper 37, 1972: D.W. Norman



ANNEX. I
Page 2
traditional manner, they have been arranged in rank order to provide an
estimate of probability. Thus the modal value (half above and half below)
provides a demonstrable 50% probability not normally associated with arithmetic
averages. However, for a basically subsistence agriculture a more stringent
requirement is appropriate and an 80% probability has been chosen. At this
probability rainfall is still quite adequate for crop growth, and even April
can expect 0.8 inches. Rainfall distribution is critical to favorable pro-
duction, and the bumper crop years have always been associated with seasons
with extended rainfall at the beginning and end of season. Basically the
farmer is a pretty busy fellow who has limited resources for carrying out his
land preparation and subsequent cultivation. Thus eight years out of ten he
can expect sufficient rain in April to allow timely cultivations and subsequent
planting operations (see Table 5). If the sequence is broken then late planting
particularly of cotton and sorghum occurs which result in low yields. Thus
provision of ox drawn equipment should allow for more timely land preparation.
At the end of the season, sometimes in September, and certainly in October,
it is important to conserve soil moisture and reduce water runoff. Thus
water conservation measures such as contour bunding and the development of
better ground cover (such as closer planting of groundnuts) are all techniques
that should be encouraged. A progressive farmer, if taking such measures,
should have little difficulty in improving his level of output.
C. Farming Systems
5.        Although no detailed surveys have been carried out, field observ-
ations tend to support the findings of the Norman study 1/ which indicate a
mean farm size of 3.9 ha. This is somewhat larger than the size originally
estimated by staff of MAR, which is thought to be biased heavily towards
the relatively densely farmed area near Funtua.
Size of Farm Family
6.        Norman 1/ reports an average farm family of 8.5.  This is not
considered to be typical of the project area, where local opinion and published
statistics point to an average farm family approximating 6.5. Composition
is assumed to be as follows:
Small          Large          Female         Male         Total
Child          Child         Adult          Adult       Residents
1.7            1.0           2.2             1.6          6.5

equivalents used by Norman 1/ would be as follows:

average farm family, daily labor availability would approximate 2.0 mandays.
8.        Labor requirements assessed by Norman 1/ on traditional methods of
cultivation, which imply mixed cropping and low yields, appear to be low
relative to data on similar crops grown under comparable conditions in other
areas. Substantially higher labor requirements have been assumed for proposed
project crops as shown in the model crop budgets (Tables 6 through 10).
9.        Adequate labor profiles are not available and, while it appears
that total labor availability (at 2.0 manday equivalents per day) is adequate
for the average small farm there may be seasonal labor shortages, particularly
at planting and weeding periods in some peasons. However, the project would
appeal primarily to the more vigorous and efficient farmers and for this reason,
labor availability is not regarded as a serious constraint to development.
Cropping Patterns
10.       Intercropping is commonly practiced in a wide variety of combin-
ations. The cropping pattern of a "typical" farm, on the basis of adjusted
areas of sole crops might be as follows (ha):
Sorghum                         1.20

Cotton                         0.90
Groundnuts & Cowpeas           0.45
Others & Fallow                0.85
Total                  3.90
11.       This pattern may be expected to change as a result of the project
which would promote sole cropping and the introduction of improved production
practices for cotton, sorghum, maize, groundnuts and cowpeas, and would result
in changes in areas planted to different crops. Overall farm size would not
be affected by the project, however the amount of land in fallow each year

12.       The anticipated effect of the project on the five crops selected

D. Cotton
Background
13.       Cotton of the improved Allen type (Gossypium hirsutum) has been
grown in the nroject area for the past half century. Production fluctuates
widely due to the effects of climate and producer price. The mean level in
recent years approximates 24,000 tons of seed cotton, some 16 percent of
mean total production from the northern States (Table 1), and 83 percent of
Katsina Province production.
14.       Seed of improved varieties produced at the Institute of Agricultural
Research (IAR) at Samaru is bulked up at the Daudawa seed multiplication farm
(see map) before distribution to farmers throughout the northern States.
15.       Seed supplies are provided free by the NSMB at a series of dumps
throughout the project area. Seed for planting is treated at the ginnery
against Bacterial Blight (Xanthomonanas malvacearum) which is endemic to the
area. Any one of three dressings: mercurial (3 percent) at 1:180; copper
(45 percent) at 1:180; or Bronopal (12 percent) at 1:170, are effective and
mav be used.
Variety
16.       The present variety, S71 has been selected to yield highly and
to meet the needs of the textile industry. UJnder farmers' conditions,
yields average no more that 240 kg of seed cotton per ha, about 10% of the
variety's yield potential. Its main characteristics are shown below:
Ginning percentage                34 to 36
Fibre length (32nds of an inch) 31 to 33
Fibre strength (g per ton)         20.9
Yarn strength (40's) CSP /1       2386
/1   Count Strength Product
Recommended Production Practices
17.      RPesearch carried out by IAR at Samaru and at outstations has given
rise to a set of recommended practices for the crop. These presuppose a low
level of technical assistance to farmers and do not reflect optimal production
conditions. More advanced techniques including the use of ultra low volume
(ULV) spraying for insect control are being tested under farmers' conditions
at several sites in the project area. They are likely to be fully approved
for use on a field scale under the conditions proposed for the project in
the near future.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
18.       Land should be ridged on the contour at 0.91 m (3 ft) centers,
and 20-25 cm (8-10 in) high. Ridges should be completed to allow for planting
in mid June. 6-8 seeds should be sown 2.4 cm (1 in) deep at 45 cm (18 in)
along the top of each ridge. 100 kg of boronated single superphosphate should
be applied broadcast before ridging (0.7 kg/ha of boron). Three weeks after
planting, 100 kg of calcium ammonium nitrate per ha should be applied close
to planting holes. Plants should be thinned to two per stand about three
weeks after planting and preferably after a good rain, to minimize the effects
of root disturbance. Weeding and ridge maintenance should be undertaken before
weed competition is serious. Normally three weedings are required.
19.       The cotton crop is attacked by a range of pests at different
stages in the growing period.
(a) Period between germination and flowering:
No major pests, Aphis gossypii may occur but seldom
build up to serious proportions.  Empoasca spp occur
but as the cotton is bred for jassid resistance no
serious damage is caused.
(b) Period of flowering and boll growth:
Pests of vegetation include the mirid Campylomma which
can be serious, Lygus which is less serious, Sylepta
derogata (F) (tlhe cotton leaf roller), various grass-
hoppers, leaf hoppers, and leaf eating bettles occur in
August and September and are of varying seriousness
depending on the season. Pests of the flowers and bolls
include stainers, mainly Dysdercus superstitiousus (F)
but also D. Fasciatus, and the red bollworm Diparop8is
watersi (Roths) which are usually more serious than the
American bollworm lleliothis armigera (Hb), the spiny
bollworm Earias insulana (Boisd) and Earias biplaga (Wlk),
the False codling moth Cryptophlebia leucotreta (5eyr) and
the pink bollworm Pectinophora gossypiella (Sound).
(c) Pests of bolls after the rains:
Bollworms (particularly the red) and adult stainers
increase until the bolls have matured.
20.       Ideally insecticidal applications should be timed according to
pest identification in the field. Since spotting of eggs demands considerable
skill and where pest identification may not occur, a precautionary spray start-
ing at 6 weeks after germination would ensure the protection of the earlier
bolls. Thereafter a succession of sprays would be undertaken and it is likely
that the advanced farmers wqould repeat the sprayings about 6 - 8 times.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
21.       Harvesting should begin in October and be continued at regular
intervals to prevent boll spoilage. Picking should aim to avoid dirt, trash
and stained or damaged lint in order to qualify seed cotton for purchase as
Grade 1. All plants have to be uprooted and burnt by March 1, and no cotton
can be planted until June 1.
Project Proposals
22.       Increased production is expected to derive partly from increased
area planted but mainly from the adoption of new techniques.
23.       Area expansion over the project period is expected to be from 90,000
ha in 1975 to 100,000 ha in 1979, an increase of 2.5 percent annually. This
would come mainly from natural population growth but there would be some area
redistribution. It is estimated that the average farmer currently grows 0.9
ha of cotton. At the end of the project period those using "advanced" tech-
niques are expected to handle about an average of 1.3 ha; those using inter-
mediate techniques an average of 1.0 ha; and those continuing to use tradi-
tional methods, 0.7 ha, providing an overall average of 1.26 ha per farmer.
24.       Adoption of new techniques would be at two levels.  The use of
early planting, sole cropping, fertilizer and ULV spraying for insect control
is described as "advanced."  Such measures would result in yields averaging
1,100 kg seed cotton per ha.  The intermediate grower with more limited
resources would be unlikely to plant near the optimum date and is expected
to use about half of the inputs of the advanced farmer and would average
Kg 720/ha.  Currently little or no spraying is carried out due to lack of
supply of inputs and very limited and sparsely located suppliers of water
required for the conventional high volume water based insecticides. During
the last three seasons ultra low volume sprayers (ULV) have been tested within
the project area and have proven successful.  They are also being used in an
IDA financed project in Malawi, as well as in other East African countries
with success. In East Africa the option for the use of high volume sprayers
exist; in the project area, due to difficulties in locating good groundwater
any large expansion of production using high volume spraying teclniques is
out of the question. During the 1971/72 season in Mozambique 11,000 ha of
VLV sprayed cotton averaged Kg 666/ha as against 36,000 ha of unsprayed cotton
averaging 262 Kg/ha. The insecticide to be used would be endosulphan, and
would be applied at a rate of 3 liter/ha per spray.
25.       Majority of farmers plant late and as such even under ideal condi-
tions of management the cotton crop would not yield at optimum levels since
the top bolls would not set. However, in order to assure the setting of
the bottom bolls it is essential that the farmer commences spraying before
the flower buds appear. As a crop insurance this should be at 5 weeks after
germination and continue at 10 day intervals for as much as 6 weeks, but
depending on the likelihood of continued crop production confined almost
entirely to the setting of the bottom bolls. The following table sets out
the proposed levels of technology:

E. Sorghum
Background
26.       Sharing with bulrush millet, 1/ the role of the traditional grain
staple of the Hausa people, sorghum is grown widely throughout the project
area.  White to yellowish grained, open headed Guinea race types, known
locally as Farafara, predominate. The grain is normally ground to a coarse
flour and eaten as porridge. The stalks, which are seldom less than four
meters in height, are used for a variety of purposes of which building and
fencing are among the most important.
27.       Under the traditional system of cultivation, mean yields are said
to approximate 750 kg of threshed grain per ha, but they vary widely in
response to intensity of intercropping, soil and climatic effects. No data
are available on sorghum production in the project area. Nevertheless, it
is safe to conclude that the crop is grown on all holdings, alone or in a
variety of mixtures.
1/   Research work to date has yielded little information on methods for
improving yields of millet, and existing varieties do not show economic
responses to fertilizer, therefore the project would not at this stage
take action to improve the production of millet.



ANNEX 1
Page 9
28.       Norman's studies 1/ indicate that sorghum occupied approximately
30% of total area planted and, whilst most commonly grown in a two crop
combination with bulrush millet, was also planted alone or with up to four
additional crops. Using this as a basis, it can be calculated that some
120,000 ha of sorghum are planted in the project area which, at an average
yield of 750 kg/ha, would produce some 90,000 tons or about 9% of estimated
production from North Central State.
29.       Although its effects have not been quantified, the parasitic weed
Striga hermontheca is probably the most significant pest of sorghum. Others
include a range of stalk borers, but particularly Busseola fusca, and Sorghum
'lidge Contarinia sorghicola. Mlajor diseases include Downy Mildew, Sclerospora
sorghi, a variety of leaf diseases causing spotting and striping, and a number
of smut diseases, mainly of the genus Sphacelotheca. The latter are to a
considerable extent controlled by seed dressing with copper fungicides which
are readily available commercially.
30.       As befits a staple grain, sorghum is normally planted on the main
farm in close proximity to the homestead. Prior to planting land is plowed
and ridged by hand hoe or occasionally mechanically, at approximately 0.9
meters between rows and varying distances within rows according to inter-
cropping practices. Sorghum is planted in the project area in Ntay/June,
after such crops as early millet.  Two or three weedings and earthings-up
are normally carried out. The crop flowers at the onset of the dry season,
in late September/early October, and matures, utilizing residual soil moisture,
by late November/December.
31.       The stalks are cut near ground level and stacked in the field after
removal of the heads, which are hundled and stored in mud plastered granaries
(rumbus) in the farm compound. Heads are removed from the granaries as
required for domestic consumption or for sale following threshing. Storage
losses are variable, one estimate is that on average 20% of the crop is lost
in store, and though control methods involving granary sanitation and the use
of insecticides liave been developed, they are not commonly used.
Recommended Practices
32.       Research on sorghum has been carried out in the northern States of
Nigeria for the past 20 years. Higher yielding varieties have been selected
from local types. A number of dwarf varieties and hybrids have also been
developed. This work has been accompanied by investigations including time
of planting, spacing, and fertilizer responses. Improved local varieties
grown under improved agronomic practices showed yield gains over traditional
types of six to 25 percent (averaging 18.6% or about 240 kg/ha) in over 90
trials planted at different sites in main sorghum production areas up to 1970.
Such increases are of little interest to farmers. The main thrust of sorghum
research is now directed towards production of high yielding dwarf varieties
1/   Op cit.



ANNEX 1
Page 10
and lhybrids. '.laterial now available has a yield potential under field condi-
tions of around 2,500 kg/ha and 4,000 kg/ha from dwarf varieties and hybrids
respectively. The grain from these types is not yet widely acceptable for
human food in rural areas but it can be sold readily in urban areas for human
and livestock food.
Project Proposals
33.       Total sorghum production in Nigeria has been estimated to approx-
imate four million tons per year, of which between five and ten percent is
marketed. Prices have risen overall in the past three years. However, any
major production increase could be expected to result in a marked fall in
price. This situation is unlikely to arise in the near future - in the light
of the ineffectiveness of existing extension, input supply and credit services
in the northern States as a whole. Within the proposed project area, however,
there are good opportunities for promoting some increase in sorghum produc-
tion, utilizing available technology and proposed project services, despite
the fact that many farmers are likely to continue to grow at least some local
sorghum under traditional cultivation methods. In addition to the switch from
traditional to improved varieties of sorghum that is envisaged, it is also
believed that a substantial amount of land which in the past has been used
to grow sorghum will, under the project, be used for maize, see para 38.
34.       Two levels of improved technology have been assumed, based upon
results from field trials and research recommendations.

F. Maize
Background
36.       Although well known, maize is presently a minor crop in the project
area. Cultivated mainly in crop mixtures on swampy areas (fadamas) primarily
as a vegetable for eating green - boiled or baked on the cob - it is unlikely
that total area exceeds 5,000 ha.
37.       High yielding, short season maize varieties and hybrids have been
developed recently by IAR and other researchers in the northern States. Their
promising performance under Northern Guinea zone conditions have created marked
interest in maize as a grain crop for the area. Trials at Daudawa under dif-
ferent levels of plant population and fertilizer rates using variety (096)
Bulk 3 gave yields ranging from 2,000 to over 7,000 kg/ha of threshed grain
depending on the level of treatments applied. Responses under farmers'
conditions in adjoining Zaria province in 1969/71 ranged from 1,400 through
2,400 to 4,000 kg/ha using comparable treatments. Planting material with
further improved yield potential is in course of development and is likely to
be available for multiplication and distribution to farmers within two years.
Project Proposals
38.       These facts and the observation that the yellow grained varieties
produce meal which is an acceptable (some say a preferred) substitute for
sorghum meal in traditional porridge in North Central State, indicate that
the probability that the maize area and production could be increased markedly
under the project. Recent trends in maize prices, the burgeoning urban popu-
lation in the southern States, and the increasing demand for livestock feeds
and for industrial maize products provide further justification for maize
promotion under the project. Probable area increases are expected to be at
the expense of sorghum and millet, and would occur mainly on the farms of
more progressive farmers. An expected increase in maize area from 5,000 to
32,500 ha of which 7,500 ha would be grown at the advanced levels on plots
averaging 1 ha, 20,000 ha (61 percent) would be grown under intermediate
technology on 0.5 ha plots and 5,000 ha (15 percent) would be grown under
traditional systems on 0.25 ha plots. About 20,000 farmers are expected to
grow maize within the project (Table 8 shows per hectare budget).
39.       Levels of technology selected for application in the project area
have been based on field trials and practices advocated by workers at the
IAR. They are summarized in the following table.

G. Groundnuts
Background
40.       Groundnuts (Arachis hypogea) are grown throughout the project area
but are of greatest commercial importance in drier, northern locations which
are climatically.less well suited to cotton production. Production levels
may be inferred from purchases by the NSMB which have averaged 14,000 tons
annually in recent years (Table 2).
41.       The Nigerian groundnut crop is grown primarily for oil and average
kernels contain about 47% oil with a Free Fatty Acid (FFA) content of 2 to
3 percent. Yields of groundnuts grown under traditional systems, which include
multiple intercropping, wide spacing and late planting, vary considerably but
are probably of the order of 480 kg/ha of shelled nuts. At this yield level
and accepting the above production assumption, area planted, on a sole crop
basis is about 32,000 ha. The high labor requirement and the present com-
Paratively unattractive returns from the crop probably limit groundnut plot
sizes to about 0.5 ha on farms which grow the crop for sale to the NSMB.
42.       A long series of agronomic trials and a number of selection and
breeding programs by staff of the IAR have resulted in the formulation of a
package of recommended practices for groundnut production. These regularly
lead to a doubling of kernel yields over traditional practices. M1ore advanced



ANNEX 1
Page 13
technologv, including the use of fungicidal sprays to control cercospora leaf
soot disease is still under investigation and may be available for dissemina-
tion in the near future. However, a number of questions still attach to the
teclhnical and economic viability of certain advanced techniques.
43.       For these reasons, only one level of improved technology has been
suggested. This approximates to the practices recommended by ERLS. The
practices proposed, vwhich are outlined below, would be fostered by the pro-
vision of a physical package of inputs including seed, seed dressing, fertilizer
and advice. This would follow the lines of a successful groundnut seed pro-
duction project in neighboring Kano State.
44.       The proposed level of improved technology would incorporate timely
planting, i.e. shortly after the first rains, on land which has been prepared
as for cotton (para 18). Fertilizer, single superphosphate at 75 kg per ha,
should he applied as a band in the old furrows before splitting to make new
ridges. Seed would be of the variety S61 which should be shelled shortly
before planting and dressed with Aldran T or Fernasan D against seed and soil
borne diseases. Seed should be planted at two every 23 cm (9 in) on the top
of the ridges i.e., a seed rate of 40 kg/ha. By so doing, the foliar cover
achieved would be invaluable in preventing run off during the heavy rainfall
in August. Furthermore, after the early weeding and earthing, such ground
cover would reduce the frequency of weeding which must cease once pegging 1/
starts. Harvesting should begin immediately the crop reaches maturity. Pod
damage should be avoided by careful lifting. The crop should be dried by
inverting plants on the ridge i.e. with pods uppermost for about six days.
Thereafter the pods should be handpicked and transported to an appropriate
site for decortication. Care is needed, particularly in lifting, drying and
picking to avoid the creation of conditions favoring the development of the
fungus Aspergillus flavus, responsible for the carcingogen, aflatoxin, which
has been transmitted on mouldv groundnuts.
Project Proposals
45.       The improved groundnut technology proposed for the project is already
well established. Its rate of spread has been limited largely by the in-
adequacy of technical assistance and input supply services. For these reasons,
the rate of uptake under the project is expected to be rapid. By Year 5 (1979),
some 30,000 farmers are expected to be using the improved technology.

H. Cowpeas
Background
46.       Cowpeas (vigna unguiculata) occupy an important place in traditional
Nigerian diets both in the North and South. They provide valuable vegetable
protein for subsistence farmers and some cash income when surplus production
is sold. The crop is seldom planted sole and normally forms one part of a
number of crop mixtures of varying complexity. Under these circumstances
yields vary widely. Further, because of the range of insect pests to which
it is susceptible both before and after flowering, yields rarely average more
than 200 kg/ha of shelled peas. Local varieties have both upright and spreading
characteristics; those producing a pale (whitish) colored seed are preferred.
47.       Research on the crop has isolated major insect pests and established
the importance of their control for increased production. Major pests include
Ootheca mutabilis, a leaf eating beetle, Alcidodes leucogrammus, a weevil
which, in both the adult and larval forms, causes stem girdling, Maruca
testulalis, the larva of which bore into and feed on young flowers causing
them to drop, Rezotrachelus varium, a weevil which lays its eggs in green pods
wherein the larvae feed and pupate within the seeds. Further, a number of
bugs in the order Hemiptera feed on green pods causing premature drying and
shrivelling.
48.       Improved practices, including sole cropping, the use of improved
upright varieties, phosphatic fertilizer and insecticidal spraying result in
ten fold yield increases over traditional yields. The latter treatment on
sole cropped cowpeas is the most significant. Present recommendations are
for six weekly sprays containing 20 percent DDT and two percent gamma BHC at
the rate of 5.5 liters of concentrate mixed in 109 liters of water per ha.
This recommendation is under review following investigation of ULV techniques.
Project Proposals
49.       No data are available on the area presently planted with cowpeas
but it is unlikely that the crop is grown widely in sole stand. The project
would introduce cowneas as a sole crop to those farmers who wished to grow
cotton using improved and advanced techniques since such farmers would already
own IJLV sprayers and be competent in their use. It is not expected that large
plots would be planted, and a mean size of 0.25 ha has been assumed.
50.       One level of technology is proposed.  Its main element is summarized
below and is compared with the traditional system.

Average yield            200                  900
shelled peas (kg/ha)
51.       It is expected that uptake of improved technology would closely
follow cotton development since materials, equipment and techniques for
spraying cowpeas approximate those used for cotton, and the benefits are
considerable. For these reasons it is expected that most farmers with one
season's experience of spraying cotton would also grow cowpeas with project
advice and assistance. Furthermore, a small proportion could be expected to
introduce improved technology to their cotton and cowpea crops simultaneously
(for detailed budget and crop projections see Tables 10 and 15).
I. Summary
52.       Absence of basic data on actual farming systems, let alone the
profitability from individual crops, severely hampers assessment of project
effects on a farm family income and therefore no attempt has been made to
formulate model farm budgets for before and after farm development, since
they would be purely hypothetical and as such could lead to wrong conclusions.
Instead comparative data on a per hectare basis is shown in the following
tables.

ANNEX 2
Poqe 1
NIGERIA
NORTI CENTRAL STATE
Funtua Agricultural Development Project
Project area, institutions and existing processing and marketing
A. Project Area
1.        Location.  Occupying a rectangular block of land between latitudes
11010' and 12°20'N and longitudes 6050' and 7°50'E, the project area covers
some 7,500 km2 or about 10% of the total area of the State. It comprises
the whole of the Funtua Administrative Division of Katsina Province in the
northern half of NCS, which includes the five southernmost districts of the
Katsina emirate. 1/ Map shows project area location within Nigeria.
2.        Climate.  Annual rainfall, confined almost entirely to the 6 months
April through September, is of the order of 1,100 mm (43 ins) with a 75 per-
cent probability of obtaining 1,000 mm. Between October and March, there is
virtually no rain; this long, dry season is rendered very harsh by hot dry
northeasterly "harmattan" winds blowing from the Sahara.
3.        Beginning in April, when there is a 50 percent chance of obtaining
35 mm of rain (1.4 in), through September which has a 50 percent probability
of more than 215 mm (8.5 in), rainfall distribution is well suited to a range
of annual crops. However, due to limitations in timely land preparations and
to a labor bottleneck at planting time, few farmers can plant all their crops
at the optimum time. Food crops are given priority over cash crops, and the
success of the latter is dependent on the farmer's residual input resources,
and because they are planted late, on the vagaries of climate; an early end to
the rainy season often vitiates most of the work put into these crops. The
problems are discussed in greater detail in Annex 1.
4.        Water.  There are few perennial streams in the area, and ground-
water supplies are severely limited by the presence of the impervious under-
lying basement complex. With increasing pressure on land, the flow period
of streams has diminished markedly. Wells commonly fail during the dry
months, leading to prolonged water shortages which are becoming an increas-
ingly critical issue for both human and livestock populations. Fortunately,
topography is such that construction of small reservoirs appears a practicable
solution to the water problem.
1/   The Emir of Katsina is the hereditary ruler of the project area.  Although
his legal powers have waned in recent years, he continues to wield
considerable influence.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
5.        Topography and Soils.  The area is underlain by rocks of the
basement complex, which is predominantly granite.  The landform is gently
undulating at an altitude of 600 to 900 m with occasional granite inselbergs.
Many small, ephemeral streams occur throughout the area.
6.        Leached ferruginous tropical soils are common with well developed
sub-soil horizons containing some 30% clay. Drainage ranges from good to
rather poor. In the southern half of the area, silt content is high which
leads to surface capping.
7.        Soils are weakly acid, with pH in the range 5.5 to 6.5 and are
deficient in nitrogen and available phosphate, particularly after prolonged
cropping. Attempts to maintain the fertility of farms close to settlements
is made by using animal manure at between two to three tons per ha. Recent
investigations have demonstrated an apparent boron deficiency and its solu-
tion, by the use of boronated superphosphate. Fertilizer responses by most
major crops have been established with some precision at low application
rates.  Field trials involving higher levels of application, mainly for
cereals such as maize and sorghum, are presently underway in the project
area.
8.        Vegetation.  Natural vegetation is savanna woodland typified by
Isoberlinia doka in association with grasses mainly of Andropogon and
Hyparrhenia species. Over much of the area the savanna has been replaced
following clearing and farming, bv parkland dominated by such trees as
Parkia clappertoniana and Adansonia digitata (Baobab). These trees are
protected by law and provide valuable dietary supplements from seeds and
leaves respectively. In wetter areas, which have been heavily farmed,
spear grass (Imperata cylindrica) is a troublesome weed. Additionally, the
parasitic weed Striga hemontheca causes significant yield reductions in
sorghum.
9.        Population.  The project area is occupied by people of the Hausa
tribe, most of whom are devout moslems. Literacy in English, Nigeria's of-
ficial language, and Hausa, the vernacular, is low among adults in rural
areas, but is rising rapidly with the younger generation. Available data
are scant. The last census, from which data are available was carried out
in 1963 and remains the best source of population data until the results of
the December 1973 census become available in detail later in 1974. Such
data will be of immeasurable value in detailed project planning and evaluation.
The only other possible source available at present, Local Authority returns
on numbers of tax payers, whilst collected annually vary in accuracy in
accordance with the diligence of the different officials charged with tax
collection responsibilities in the districts. Information from such sources
indicates only 60-80 percent of the adult male population that has been
imputed from census data. According to the census, population of the five
districts constituting the project area totalled 698,748 in 1963. Using a
growth rate of 2.5 percent annually, population of the project area currently
should be about 905,000.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
10.       Data on numbers of persons per family in the project area are
also sparse. No demographic surveys have been carried out. However,
indications from tax data point to an average of 4.22. Local opinion
does not confirm this, suggesting that family size approximates 6.5
individuals. This figure in turn is lower than that obtained in a de-
tailed survey of three villages in Zaria Province, some 60 km south of
the project area. The latter arrived at a mean figure of 8.5 persons
per family. This is substantially higher than the figure suggested by
the Federal Office of Statistics for Zaria Province as a whole. Because
of the snread between family size derived from tax data on the one hand,
and a microsample in a neighboring province on the other, 6.5 was arbi-
trarily selected as representing average family size. This was in
accordance with local opinion and falls between the extremes otherwise
suggested. Thus, on the basis of a family size of 6.5, and a population
of 905,000, there are now estimated to be some 139,230 families in the
project area.
11.       Numbers of farming families were calculated on the assumption
that about 28 percent would be engaged primarily in non-agricultural
activities. This figure was derived from census data and observations
on non-agricultural activities in the area. On this basis, it is cur-
rently estimated that there are about 100,000 farming families in the
project area.
12.       The population is divided between five districts of Funtua Division
and resides in 77 village areas. The estimated situation in 1974 is as
shown below:
District                No. Village Area            Population
Funtua                         16                    229,000
Bakori                         19                    233,000
Malumfashi                     22                    228,000
Kankara                        12                    132,500
Faskari                         8                     82,500
TOTAL                  77                     905,000
13.       The social structure of the area is significant because of the
relative strength and influence of the hierarchical structure of the Local
Authority. The project comes within the jurisdiction of the Emir of Katsina
whose hereditary authority is delegated through salaried district heads and
village heads.
14.       Among their other functions, village heads are responsible for
village level administration of land tenure arrangements. The system is
based on Islamic law and on customary usufructory rights. In theory, it
provides for the allocation of such rights to land which is not yet so
allocated, to any native Nigerian who expresses the intention of settling
and using land within the village area. In practice, most land has been



ANNEX 2
Page 4
allocated to families, the heads of which suballocate to family members in
accordance with assessed need and capability.
15.       As representatives of the Local Authority, village heads have
considerable political influence within their areas. Although this influence
is being eroded in urban areas, such as the wards of Funtua town, it is still
considerable in the villages. For this reason, every effort would be made to
obtain the support of village heads for the project by acquainting them with
proposed and ongoing developments within their areas. District heads would
be similarly consulted and informed. Furthermore, as indicated in Chapter VI,
provision is made for Local Authority representation on the Project Committee.
16.       Farm Size.  Information on farm size is scant.  It is possible to
impute a possible mean farm size on the basis of assumed population, family
size and estimated area of cultivated land. The latter figure is estimated
by NCS at 390,000 ha or 52% of total project area. Given 100,000 farm
families (para 3.03), then average size of cultivated holding, i.e. exclud-
ing fallow, would be 3.9 ha. This figure is in reasonable accord with mean
farm size established by a detailed survey in a similar area some 60 km to
the south.
17.       There are some very large farms within the area.  These farms
would normally receive credit through commercial banks; the project would
help such large farmers by employing a farm management specialist to advise
them.
18.       Cropping Patterns.  Cropping patterns are dominated by sorghum and
millet as food grains, and cotton and groundnuts as the major cash crops. A
diversity of minor crops are grown. Local knowledge and socio-economic sur-
veys in neighboring parts of the northern States indicate that sorghum and
millet typically occupy 45-55% of cultivated areas, and cotton about 25-30%
in the southern two-thirds of the area. In the drier north, cotton tends to
give way to groundnuts. Minor crops, usually grown in mixtures with the
main cereals and cash crops, include maize, chilies, kenaf, okra and a range
of vegetables.
19.       Communications.  The project area is well served by main roads
(see map). Additionally, the Nigerian Railway Corporation has a spur line
from its main northern route, that runs from Zaria via Funtua to Kaura
Namoda in North West State. A major road runs parallel with the line through
the south and west of the project area.
20.       Unfortunately, the basic communication network barely extends be-
yond that described above. Minor roads are non-existent or in poor repair.
Communities are seasonally cut off from each other and the outside world.
Transportation of produce and inputs to rural areas is both difficult and
expensive. Attempts to improve the situation by self help activities and
by the Local Authority Works Department are hampered by shortages of funds
and executive capacity.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
B. Institutions
21.       Institutions that would be concerned with the project and their
relevant responsibilities are described in the following paragraphs.
22.       The Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR).  MANR would
have responsibility for carrying out the project. The Ministry has representa-
tives of its four technical divisions within the project area, and it operates
cotton seed multiplication farms at Daudawa and Malumfashi (see map). While
the functions of its Field Services and Produce Inspection Divisions within
the area would be incorporated into the project organization, the administration
of the two other Divisions would remain unchanged. Thus, animal health, organ-
ization of grazing facilities 1/ and livestock improvement services, largely
for the semi-nomadic Fulani pastoralists, would continue to be provided by
the Veterinary Division; and the Forestry Division would continue its work,
which is concerned mainly with the regulation of reserved land, and planting
of woodlots. These functions would be largely unaffected by the project.
23.       The Ministry of Works (MOW).  Responsible for State-financed public
works program, MOW has limited facilities and a small technical staff deployed
within the project area; MOW is concerned mainly with road maintenance,
building construction, and the maintenance of State-financed structures.
Executive capacity relative to the State construction program is a major
limitant to its operations.
24.       The Ministry of Health (MOH).  Responsible for all aspects of human
health in the State, MOH is in the process of developing its services and
facilities. A Rural Health Center to provide simple treatment services for
both in and out-patients has been completed at Funtua. This will provide
the referral link between the sparse network of district dispensaries and
the specialized hospital facilities operated by MOH and Ahmadu Bello Uni-
versity (ABU) at Zaria and the State Capital, Kaduna. Additional Rural
Health Centers and a closer network of dispensaries providing more compre-
hensive services are to be included in the forthcoming development plan
(1974-1978).
25.       Ministry of Industry Trade, and Cooperatives (MITC) provides
services to industrial investors and is involved, for example, with the
development of the new oil seed crushing mill being constructed at Funtua
1/   This aspect would be affected by the Bank's proposed Livestock Project
appraisal report 127 UNI which includes provision for improvement of
facilities and services to pastoralists utilizing a grazing reserve
in the north west corner of the project area.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
with partial IFC financing 1/. More relevantly, it is responsible for the
administration of Cooperative societies within the State, which has 420
primary societies affiliated to 9 cooperative unions. 350 of these societies
are agricultural cooperatives primarily engaged in crop marketing and input
supply.
26.       The Katsina Local Authority (KLA).  The project area comes under
the control of the KLA, whose main functions include the collection of tax
in respect of people and cattle (haraji and jangali respectively); the ad-
ministration of land allocation; and the provision of services such as
feeder road construction and maintenance, dispensaries, and primary educa-
tion as a complement to State government activities in these fields.
27.       Two functions of the KLA are of immediate and direct importance
to the project. The first relates to the need for the positive and open
support and encouragement of project activities by KLA and the close coopera-
tion between KLA officials and project management. Project concept and pro-
posed activities have been discussed and agreed at several levels with the
KLA including the Emir, and full cooperation is anticipated. The second
concerns maintenance of the road network to be constructed under the proj-
ect, a responsibility for which KLA would ultimately assume. The road
construction unit of the project would liaise closely with the KLA Works
Department in order to facilitate road maintenance.
28.       Funtua Cooperative Credit and Marketing Union Ltd.  The Union
covers the entire project area and initially comprised 122 affiliated primary
societies. During the 1960's, cooperatives suffered from lack of support and
supervisory staff, alleged corruption, and political interference. Loans to
farmers, usually in cash, were made without regard to capabilities of
borrowers or to availability of inputs. Borrowers were provided with in-
adequate technical supervision and consequently loans were often misused and
repayments were poor. Many societies failed, and in 1973, only 41 active
societies with 3,400 members remained. In 1973 the Union loaned N79,000.
29.       The Union reports that 51 new societies are being organized; in
addition, there are 76 unaffiliated cooperatives that cannot receive Gov-
ernment loan funds due to indebtedness and default. Efforts are being made
to revitalize these societies.  Currently, however, societies serve less
than 5% of project area farmers and in general are not popular. To the ex-
tent that cooperative societies are vital and efficient, project management
would work with them as channels for credit, inputs and marketing.
30.       The Rural Development Bureau (RDB).  The RDB is a State government
body composed of representatives of public entities concerned with the rural
sector. The Bureau has a relatively informal role in coordinating the rural
development aspects of State sponsored programs. At the same time it is
responsible for the implementation of a number of civil works construction
projects utilizing its small budget, and contributions from rural communities.
1/   Report IFC/P 138 January 10, 1973.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
Activities center mainly on construction of access roads, wells and earth
dams for rural water supply. RDB has a small technical staff and is largely
limited in its endeavors by this constraint. During the project development
period, RDB would continue its important functions, concentrating its ef-
forts outside the project area.
31.       The Institute of Agricultural Research (IAR).  Created in the
early 1960's from the Research Division of the then Northern Nigeria Ministry
of Agriculture, IAR is a semi-autonomous body linked with Ahmadu Bello
University (ABU). Its governing body has representation from the Ministries
of Agriculture and Natural Resources of the six northern States. Its main
research station is located at Samaru, near Zaria, some 60 km from Funtua.
Further details on the organization and output of IAR are in Annex 3.
32.       IAR preserves contacts with headquarters and field staff of MANR.
Field trials are carried out on an agency basis on MANR sites, supervised by
ministry staff under the technical guidance of IAR researchers. Research
recommendations are conveyed to extension staff via the Extension Research
Liaison Service (ERLS) of IAR.
33.       While IAR has directed a major part of its resources to fundamental
plant breeding in the search for improved high yielding varieties, which
must continue, it must do more to find solutions to the practical everyday
problems of small farmers who struggle with seasonal limitations of labor and
severe shortages of capital and other resources. A practical program of
applied research aimed at identifying and resolving farmers' problems would
make IAR's program more immediately useful to the people it is intended to
serve.
34.       Nigerian Agricultural Bank (NAB).  NAB is a Federal Government
institution which was inaugurated in March 1973, with head offices in
Kaduna. UNDP has provided financial assistance to employ consultants to
plan the role of NAB and to manage it for three years; the Bank is executive
agency. Four expatriate senior management staff, including the General
Manager, have been employed; and Nigerian officers have been recruited.
35.       Northern States Marketing Board (NSMB).  NSMB is responsible for
organizing the domestic purchase of controlled crops, cotton and groundnuts
at prices predetermined by FGN. As from the 1973/74 season, NSMB became
purchasing agent of the Federal Nigerian Produce Marketing Company (NPMC),
under a new FGN program to improve crop purchasing. Crop purchases take
place at legally gazetted markets by Licenced Buying Agents (LBA) of NSMB.
In 1972, there were 34 cotton markets and 27 groundnut buying points with-
in the project area.
36.       Cotton and Agricultural Processors Ltd. (CAP).  CAP (formerly the
British Cotton Growers' Association (Nigeria) Ltd.) has had a monopoly for
ginning Nigerian cotton since exports began over half a century ago. CAP



ANNEX 2
Page 8
has ginneries throughout the cotton growing zones of the northern States.
In addition to ginning and lint classification, CAP arranges for seed
dressing against Bacterial Blight, seed distribution to village dumps, and
transportation of lint and seed as directed by NSMB. CAP is efficient and
capable of expanding its operations to meet the needs of the project. The
present ginning agreement expires in 1976, and CAP is exploring with State
Governments the possibilities of greater local participation in the industry.
C. Processing and Marketing
37.       Cotton.  All cotton purchased for NSIEB in the project area is
ginned at CAP facilities located either at Funtua or Malumfashi (see map).
The Malumfashi ginnery also processes cotton produced outside the project
area. Some increase in effective capacity would probably be needed towards
the end of the project, and CAP has indicated its willingness to make the
necessary arrangements, in consultation with MANR and NSMB.
38.       The gazetted cotton markets usually are temporary structures made
from grass mats, and are located on the basis of anticipated purchases
(minimum 50 tons/year) and distance between markets (minimum 8 km). A
minimum of two LBA is mandatory for each market, which is controlled by a
Produce Examiner (PE) from the Produce Inspection Division (PID) of the
MANR. The PE are responsible for the visual grading of seed cotton into
one of two grades, and should ensure that LBA pay correct amounts to farmers
for their produce. The PE are severely criticized as they seldom perform
these functions; apparently grading is virtually non-existent, and farmers
allege that many PE conspire with LBA to cheat farmers. The project would
seek to correct these deficiencies.
39.       Cotton Seed.  Currently cotton seed is dressed against fungal
infection by CAP and is returned to the farmer. In the handling processes,
enormous losses occur which could be substantially reduced were an acid
delinting process employed. Through this, the seed germination rate would
be improved, thus requiring less seed to be returned to the farmer and re-
leasing a greater surplus of seed for the crushing industry. NSMB would
request CAP to install acid delinter equipment at their gin in Funtua; CAP
have agreed in principal to this suggestion.
40.       Groundnuts.  All groundnuts purchased for NSMB are inspected and
graded by PID staff. Normally, this takes place after the purchases from
farmers have been made by LBA at buying points licensed by KLA. Groundnut
buying points like cotton markets, are not properly supervised by PID and
there are frequent allegations that LBA manipulate weigh scales and prices
at the expense of farmers.



ANNEX 2
Page 9
41.       Other Crops.  Control is normally not exercised on the marketing
or handling of other crops, such as sorghum, maize and cowpeas. However,
KLA has the power, if agreed by NCSG, to restrict movement of foodcrops in
times of shortage. Such powers have been invoked in poor seasons from time
to time.
42.       There is no national food crop market as such, but rather a series
of fairly highly organized, but disconnected, local centers. Consequently,
markets are thin and marked temporal and spatial price irregularities occur.
The project would aim to improve matters by the introduction of a market in-
telligence service, increasing farmers bargaining power through crop assembly,
and providing marketing advice and where appropriate helping farmers groups
to enter into contracts with large consumers such as livestock feed pro-
ducers and commercial starch factories.

ANNEX 3
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
Funtua Agricultural Development Project
Research and Seed Multiplication
A. Research Implementation
Institution
1.        Technical and rural economic research pertaining to the proj-
ect area is the responsibility of the semi-autonomous Institute of
Agricultural Research (IAR). The Institute was created in the early
1960's and is linked to the Ahmadu Bello University (ABU) at Zaria. It
has its main station and administrative headquarters at Samaru, adjoin-
ing the ABU campus, some 60 km south of proposed project headquarters
at Funtua. IAR serves the six northern States. Senior officials from
their Ministries of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR) help to
formulate IAR policy and approve research programs.
2.        The Institute has a staff of over 150 professionals, and
modern and expanding facilities. It has established apparently good
working relations with other research institutions in Nigeria and the
West African region. Its standing in research circles is acknowledged
to be high. Over the years it has produced a wide range of research
results relating to most of the crops commonly grown in the northern
States. Implementation of the results has been hampered by lack of
technical assistance and input delivery services at the village and
farmer levels; and a tendency for research to be unrelated to the condi-
tions encountered on farmers' farms. The latter weakness has been
high-lighted recently as a result of the activities of the Agricultural
Economics Department of IAR, whose staff are cooperating with technical
personnel in the formulation of research recommendations more nearly
related to farmers' needs and capabilities than has hitherto been the
case.
3.        Responsibility for transmission of research results to exten-
sion staff is vested in the Extension Research Liaison Service (ERLS)
of IAR. Headed by a deputy Director of the Institute, ERLS produces a
range of audio and visual aids materials suitable for use by farmer con-
tact extension staff. The Service is responsible for formulating draft
recommended practices for farmers. These are based on research results
and an assessment of the level of supervision and technical competence
of field level extension staff employed by the State MANR's. This is
generally low, hence recommended practices tend to be simple and conser-
vative in order to avoid the hazard of loss of confidence by farmers in



ANNEX 3
Page 2
extension staff who give wrong advice (due to lack of comprehension) or
who are unable to arrange for supplies of necessary inputs. ERLS has a
staff of some 20 professionals and support personnel. Its chief would
be willing to tailor extension material to meet proposed project needs,
and to assist with its production.
4.        The use of IAR research results as a basis for recommendations
by ERLS to farmers has limitations. The farmer does not have the ideal
conditions for crop growth enjoyed by IAR on its stations. His resources
are limited, which necessitates a cropping pattern dictated by his lim-
ited resources. What is now urgently required is a range of experimental
work to show what can be accomplished under the conditions of crop suc-
cessions which the environment and limited manpower resources impose on
the farmer.
5.        Demonstrations, on both individual holdings and seed multipli-
cation farms (see Schedule 1) using applicable IAR recommended inputs
and improved seed varieties, would play an important part in teaching
new techniques to farmers. These demonstrations must be of a very high
standard to have a real impact.
Research Programs and Recommendations
6.        Programs on the five crops proposed for the project have been
ongoing for many years. Thus, cotton has been the subject of continuing
research in the major disciplines over the past four decades. Programs
are staffed mainly by personnel of the Cotton Research Corporation (CRC).
They have resulted in the development of improved varieties. Thus S7t,
which is recommended for the project area, has a yield potential under
Samaru conditions of well over, 2,000 kg of seed cotton per ha. Its main
characteristics are listed in Annex 1. In addition, agronomic practices,
fertilizer responses, pest and disease problems of crop have been clearly
defined and, at least, partially solved, so that farm yields of about
1,100 kg can be expected over wide areas.
7.        The cotton research program has provided definite recommenda-
tions on variety, cultural practices and fertilizer applications appli-
cable to the project area. Currently it is in the course of evaluating
systems of insect control using ultra-low-volume (ULV) spraying techniques
on a field scale at different sites in the project area. Advice on ULV
techniques suitable for farmers is likely to be forthcoming early in 1974.
These would be similar to techniques used by small scale cotton producers
in other parts of Africa. Annex 1 contains the essence of research rec-
ommendations for the cotton crop plus proposals for ULV spraying based
on experience elsewhere. Future work in the cotton research program
should include testing varieties that have a short growing period and can
thus be planted late and still have a reasonable yield expectation. Late
planting of cotton is a practice forced on many farmers because of limited
manpower resources.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
8.        Groundnuts have been the subject of close research interest
and substantial effort over the past two decades. High yielding varieties
suitable for the project area have been developed and a package of inputs
and practices capable of at least doubling farmer's yields has been
available for some years.
9.        Improved practices proposed for the project are based on cur-
rent recommendations by ERLS. They include the use of the seed of the
variety S61, seed dressing with Aldran T or Fernasan D, single super-
phosphate fertilizer at 75 kg per ha, early planting and close spacing.
It may be that ongoing research on control of cercospora leaf spot will
lead shortly to the possibility of incorporating this treatment in prac-
tices proposed for groundnuts in the project area. This should be kept
in mind, along with opportunities for production expansion through new
varieties and/or variations in fertilizer dressing, all of which are
under investigation.
10.       Sorghum research has progressed from attempts to improve local
varieties into the development of dwarf varieties and hybrids suitable
for the differing ecological conditions encountered in the northern
States. Such material has a yield potential of at least five times
greater than farmers presently obtain. Selection and breeding programs
have been underway for the past 20 years with substantial assistance in
the past decade from the US through USAID programs involving USDA, USARS
and several universities. Cooperative programs with other West African
sorghum producers have also been operated through IAR.
11.       The outcome of this work, and related agronomic investigations,
has not yet reached the stage at which ERLS is able to issue a series
of recommended practices for general distribution. However, highly
promising results have been obtained from a number of trials, replicated
at Daudawa (in the project area) and a number of other sites in ecologi-
cally comparable locations. These have formed the basis for two pro-
posed levels of technology, outlined in Annex 1, involving the use of
dwarf varieties and hybrid sorghum. Proposed levels of fertilizer
application and the specific types of sorghum most suitable for the
project area should be reviewed after 1973 and 1974 cropping seasons in
the light of results of ongoing and proposed field trials scheduled for
Daudawa and other sites. Acceptance for human consumption of new hybrid
varieties on the basis of palatability should be examined closely.
12.       Mlaize has the most exciting crop development potential in the
project. Recent research results in Nigeria, and experience in other
African countries having similar ecological conditions point to the
probability of a substantial expansion of area and production in the
short to medium term. Yields of over 7,000 kg/ha have been obtained
under trial conditions over several years at Samaru from synthetic vari-
eties and hybrids. Trials in the project area have yielded similarly,
and promising new material is presently being tested. Even if a reduc-
tion of 50 percent from research to farmer fields is assumed yield levels
of at least 3,500 kg can be readily obtained.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
13.       These developments are comparatively recent in the northern
States. They have been initiated by a number of cooperative and regional
programs, partly funded by the US. Table 2 indicates yield potential of
some of the material being developed. Agronomic practices established
elsewhere have been, and continue to be, tested under Nigerian conditions.
Some refinements are likely to be forthcoming, but basic production
techniques utilizing comparatively heavy fertilizer applications have
been proven in trials.
14.       Annex 1 sets out two levels of recommended maize technology
based on Nigerian research results and experience in other African coun-
tries having similar ecological conditions. These differ from current
recommended practices since they presuppose a much closer degree of
technical assistance to farmers plus the provision of the inputs neces-
sary to obtain predicted production levels. As for sorghum, proposed
technology should be reviewed in the light of 1973 and 1974 field trial
results and amended as necessary before incorporation in project
activities.
15.       Cowpea is grown throughout the project area, and widely in
Nigeria. Research has centered mainly on control of insect pests, to
which it is highly susceptible both in the field and in store. However,
some improved varieties have been established and recommendations are
available on cultural practices. Responses to phosphatic fertilizer
(single superphosphate) have been demonstrated.
16.       Control of insect pests is possible, provided the crop is sole
planted using high volume sprays and DDT/BHC mixtures. The probability
of insect control using ULV techniques is currently under investigation
at IAR. Results suitable for dissemination to farmers are expected to
be forthcoming in the near future.  In anticipation of this, an improved
level of technology is proposed for the project, that involves ULV
spraying and fertilizer application to sole crops planted as recommended
by ERIS. Confirmation or possibly modification of the treatment should
be sought before their implementation, following results of ongoing and
planned investigations by IAR staff.
17.       The project management would undertake field trials in close
cooperation with IAR at the Daudawa and Malumfashi farms. Investigations
would form part of ongoing research programs in major commodities in
the project area. They would pay particular attention to the applica-
bility of results under project conditions.
Seed Multiplication
18.       Seed multiplication is the responsibility of State Ministries
of Agriculture and Natural Resources from parent nucleus material devel-
oped by IAR. At the present time there is no private sector seed indus-
try. With the exception of the cotton seed multiplication scheme at



ANNEX 3
Page 5
Daudawa, Gusau and Gombe, efforts to produce and distribute improved
planting material are generally inadequate to meet the needs of the
northern States (for details of stage multiplication of cotton see
Table 1).
19.       Project proposals for a substantial uptake of improved plant-
ing material of four of the five crops considered dictate that adequate
multiplication facilities should be developed in the area. MA9NR already
has a 390 ha farm at Daudawa at which cotton seed for the State, and
other areas is bulked up. An additional farm of 210 ha at Malumfashi
in the north of the project area would also be made available for seed
multiplication.
20.       Seed production is a specialized business calling for good
management standards and a high level of technical competence. Most
KANR staff at farm manager level are weak in those qualities, and it is
questionable whether existing MANR personnel could operate a seed produc-
tion program of the size proposed for the project. Quantities of seed
would not be large initially, but would build up quite quickly as shown
in sunmary below:
____ ----TYear ----- ------------
1      2      3       4       5
m. tons
Sorghum                60    112    224    316       384
Maize                   -      20    108    325      587
Groundnuts             30    180    450    600       900
21.       Parts of both sorghum and maize seed production programs would
be of the new hybrids; exact amounts would depend on farmer uptake which
itself depends on the successful demonstration of significant yield dif-
ferences between the new hybrids and current composite strains. Ground-
nut and cotton seed production would pass through two multiplication
stages on the project seed farms; final multiplication would be on pri-
vate seed farms. The 600 ha of the two project seed farms would be adequate
to handle cotton and groundnuts up to the third stage and the cereal pro-
duction in the early years. But the use of selected commercial farmers
within the project area must be envisaged for cereal quantities such as
those projected from year 3 onwards. Seed production developments under
this project could be substantially expanded to serve as a major seed
producing location for the State.
22.       Schedule 1 of this Annex gives details on requirements, costs
and returns for Daudawa and Malumfashi seed farms.
