ANNEX 4
NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Roads
General
1.        The lack of reasonable farm access constitutes a serious constraint
to development, the rectification of which is essential for the success of
the project. To enable most farmers to be within two kilometers of a reason-
able road requires development of 1,500 km of agricultural roads in the
Funtua area. These roads are needed so that vital agricultural extension
work can reach the farmer during the wet season when his crops are growing,
and for the delivery of farm inputs and evacuation of produce during the dry
season. In addition, social benefits by way of easier and more economical
haul of other supplies, better bus services, and easier travel by the
farmer's own modes of transport such as bicycles, donkey, ox cart and
tractor, would result.
2.        Traffic densities are difficult to predict with accuracy, but it
is certain that most of the heavy lorry traffic would occur in the dry
season when haul of farm inputs and produce is at its peak. Light traffic
would continue throughout the year, mostly consisting of local movement be-
tween villages. Because of this traffic pattern, where the heavier axle
loads occur in the dry months, it should be possible to use most of the
roads throughout the year, the main obstacle being those roads intersected
by a major river crossing which could cut off traffic for several months in
the wet season. Careful road location is necessary to reduce the frequency
of such obstacles.
3.        During the five year development period, the project would con-
struct 1,500 km of agricultural roads, of which, about 1125 km would be un-
surfaced and 375 kmn would be surfaced. Roads would only be surfaced - with
appropriate thicknesses of natural occurring gravels - on main extraction
routes to market centers and where soil conditions make this essential
(see map for proposed road layout).
4.        Construction Standards. 1/ Roads would be constructed on the water-
sheds 2/ as such alignments are self draining and require minimal earthworks
Construction would involve 1,500-2,500 YC/mile over a 32 ft formation, and
would be undertaken most effectively by tractors for site clearance, and
motor graders for shaping the formation and providing turn-out drains from
1/   Basic standards and data are calculated using English measurements.
2/   Lines of separation between drainage basins.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
longitudinal side drains; the use of motor graders would predominate.
Stripping (or topsoiling) over the formation width would seldom be required
on watersheds where weathering had taken place exposing material which is
capable, without surfacing, of adequately carrying the low volumes of traf-
fic likely to use such roads. Proposed typical road cross sections are at-
tached for (a) construction by grader and (b) low embankments that would be
required where low-lying areas cannot be avoided (see Figure I of Annex 4).
5.        Construction Technique
(i)  Selection of road center line would be carefully carried out
by stereoscopic examination of aerial photography (provided for under the
project, Annex 10 Table 2) and large-scale maps, and by site inspection and
levelling to locate the tops of ridges, which are often very flat. The
alignments would slavishly follow selected watersheds.
(ii) Clearing of the formation would be carried out by tractor/
dozer/scraper combinations and debris moved outside the formation width.
(iii)  Topsoiling (or soil stripping) would only be carried out where
absolutely necessary, e.g. where topsoil or unsuitable material exists and
where the use of such soil in construction would reduce road bearing strength.
(iv)  Construction by grader.  On watershed routes, the main method
of construction would be by motor grader. Material from graderblade cut
side drains, depth 12" - 18", would be moved towards the center of the form-
ation width and shaped to a smooth camber. This is essentially a lateral
earth movement although some longitudinal regularization would occur along
the road center line as the grader shapes the formation. Scarifying the
material in the side drains may be necessary and occasionally a tractor-
mounted ripper might be needed to loosen material for easier movement by the
grader.
(v) Provision of turn-out (offshoot drains) from the longitudinal
side drains of the formation would be grader constructed to discharge water
from the formation and so prevent erosion in the side drains being caused by
long uninterrupted drainage flows. Care would be taken to ensure that turn-
out drains had the correct slope to take water away from the formation.
(vi) Compaction of formation after shaping is desirable. The sup-
ply of water for consumption by project staff and for road and dam construc-
tion would be difficult. Rivers do not flow continually throughout one year,
very few dams exist, shallow wells generally yield low, and frequently fail
in the dry season; and bore holes (tube wells) have not proved successful in
the basement complex of the project areas.
The initial stage of the construction program would be confined
for the first dry season of the project to areas within a workable range of
existing water supplies, say, 20 miles. Thereafter the dams constructed in
the first stage, after filling during the rains, would become the water
source for wider reaching road and dam construction activities.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
(vii) Surfacing would only be carried out by tractor and scraper
over sections where the subgrade is found to have inadequate bearing
strength even for low traffic volumes, and where supplies of surfacing ma-
terial within the practical working haul of tracked tractors. Scrapers
would require the support of a pusher during such operations. The principal
function of the tractors would be clearing, dozing and ripping and not sur-
facing (see para 11). The self-loading scrapers proposed for the dam con-
struction unit would assist with surfacing in the wet season. Local haulage
contractors would be used where long hauls of gravel are found necessary.
(viii) Minor embankments would be constructed by tractors and scrapers
on those sections where watershed construction is impossible. Side borrow to
fill would be used where practicable (see typical cross-sections). In adopt-
ing predominantly watershed alignments, embankments would be required in
crossing from one watershed to another and in doing so, at some sites, it
would be possible to construct dams and allow traffic to cross over a widened
dam wall.
(ix) Drainage structures. Corrugated metal pipe culverts would be
used because of the ease and speed with which these can be transported, as-
sembled and constructed. Bitumen-coating of metal pipe would be an effective
method of protecting culverts from the slightly acid (plH 5-6) soil water to
which they would be exposed. Concrete headwalls, tailwalls and drop-pits
using standardized form-work would be constructed.
Method of Procurement
6.        Road Works - Three possibilities exist:
(i)  By Contract.  It is the opinion of Federal and State MOW offi-
cials that major Nigerian (and foreign operations in Nigeria) civil engineer-
ing contractors are fully (if not over) committed with Federal and State
highway development programs and that contracts for low-standard agricultural
roads would not interest them at the present time. The project program is
not of a type to interest new foreign contractors because of their low unit
cost of construction, long construction period envisaged, and demand for a
modest amount of equipment and plant.
(ii) By State Ministries and Agencies. The State Ministries of
Works and Agriculture and Natural Resources would be unable to undertake the
construction of project roads and dams without considerable strengthening in
staff and equipment. In any case MOW is already over committed and is ex-
tremely short of adequately experienced supervisory and technical personnel.
(iii) By Project Construction Units. The establishment of these
units would involve recruitment of senior managerial, technical and
mechanical staff, procurement of plant, training of operators, setting up
workshops, costing procedures and evaluation of physical outputs. The re-
cruitment of high calibre senior personnel with experience in similar work



ANNEX 4
Page 4
is essential to the success of project units. However, the main advantage
of project construction by force account would be lower cost of construc-
tion, flexible use of equipment between roads and dams and the training of
personnel to undertake similar work elsewhere in the State.
7.        Other integrated agricultural projects financed by the Bank have
in some instances involved the development of infrastructure through project
construction units. Construction has generally been efficient, at low cost,
and has provided an excellent training ground for young indigenous engineers.
In the light of such experiences, the inadequacy of existing Nigerian insti-
tutions, the lack of available contractors, and the need for flexibility,
the project would set up a special unit to undertake the construction of
roads, dams and soil conservation measures.
Construction Costs
8.        The average overall road construction cost is estimated at about
N 1900 (US$2,900) per km. The cost includes engineering overheads, plant
capital and operating costs, workshop capital and operating costs, labor
and engineering supplies (see Tables 1-4).
Plant Requirement
9.        Plant requirements for agricultural road construction units have
been estimated by using plant output figures for similar work under similar
terrain and rainfall conditions and by estimating the total work quantities
required. Table 2 shows recommended major plant items. The predominant
methlod of construction would be by motor grader with one more powerful
grader for work in harder soil conditions. Other major equipment includes
two tracked tractors with dozers and scrapers for clearing and forming
shallow embankments, and two vibratory rollers for compaction or formation
and surfacing, where required.
Staff Requirements
10.       These have been related to the numbers and types of construction
equipment and to the tasks involved in alignment selection, grade construc-
tion, surfacing and drainage structures. A high degree of mobility is de-
sirable; caravans (dwelling trailers) would be provided for senior person-
nel and portable accommodation for artisans, operators and laborers (Annex
6 Table 3).
Road Maintenance
11.       Although some road maintenance would be undertaken by the project
during the development period, the allocation of construction plant for
maintenance would not be allowed to interfere with output of new road; thus
once a new road had been built maintenance responsibility would be handed



ANNEX 4
Page 5
over to the Katsina Local Authority (KLA). Funds have been included under
Annex 10 Table 6 for the purchase and operation of maintenance equipment,
and under the project KLA staff would, during the project, be seconded to
the project engineering service for purposes of training and operating con-
trol.
Bridges
12.       Small streams would be crossed by constructing "Irish" crossings - a
concrete slab on a suitable rock foundation - or through the use of ARMCO type
culverts. No large bridges would be constructed, since most rivers are ex-
tremely wide and run dry for 8 months of the year, at a time when crops are
being evacuated, and inputs are being delivered to service center stores. All
the roads would have year round access from at least one end so that during
the wet season access can be gained. In the dry season road maintenance units
would cut new approaches to the river beds, which would be sufficient to allow
ingress to traffic.
Compensation
13.       Compensation for loss of arable land due to road construction would
be made at established KLA rates and the amounts (provided for under Annex 10
Table 6) would be determined by KLA.

ANNEX 5
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Water and Soil Conservation
1.        The project area suffers from extreme shortage of water for
domestic purposes during the dry season and part of the wet season until
village wells start to yield properly. The area is also prone to soil
erosion principally in the catchment areas of small streams and rivers.
Under the ecological and climatic conditions prevailing in the Project
area, soil and water conservation are synonymous with each other, and where
possible would be treated together, particularly as proposed under the
project, water development would be through the construction of surface
water dams.
A. Water Conservation and Rural Water Supplies
2.        Shortage of water supplies for domestic use during the dry season
and beginning of the wet season, January through to August, is acute in
the Project area. It is estimated that the existing wells, dams and tapkis
(ponds) can serve only 10-20% of total demand. Villagers depend for their
supplies on water from wells that on average can only be dug to 10-20 m,
most of which dry up during the dry season. An alternative source of water
are tapkis and small earth dams, but these are infrequent, and at times
poorly sited and constructed, resulting in poor holding capacity and at
times breakages. The final alternative is stream bed wells. These are
seasonally hand dug in water bearing sands of some, but not all, rivers
and streams. In many instances these are last resort supplies resulting
in carry distances of up to 5 miles. Water collected from such sources is
either head or donkey carried, and during the crisis period (from February
to June) is sold at a standard rate of NO.10 (US$0.15) per 20 liters. The
lack of water is a serious social and teclnical constraint, and unless efforts
are made to conserve and improve water supplies the project might only
partially succeed.
3.        Existing plans by the State Government to improve water supplies
in rural areas has been given high budgetary priority. Plans are being
prepared to improve water supplied to larger towns, but for rural villages
efforts rarely go beyond providing assistance for well improvements. The
latter is not the correct policy since the ground water available in the



ANNEX 5
Page 2
shallow aquifer is limited, and new wells themselves dry up and accelerate
the drying up of existing wells. In short it would seem that the draw off
of ground water is greater than its reservoir supply.
4.        The possibilities of utilizing ground water from deep aquifers
have been reviewed. Unfortunately geological conditions in the area are
such that the Basement complex, which is an aquiclude, is of shallow for-
mation and precludes the possibilities of deep aquifers. Furthermore
even if utilization were possible some form of mechanical pumping would
be necessary raising problems of maintenance.  Experience shows that most
of the simple pumps installed in the past have been broken within a year
and subsequently have been rarely repaired.
5.        For the reasons outlined above heavy reliance on dugwells and/or
deep boreholes are impracticable.  Fortunately it is possible to exploit
surface water supplies and to impound wet season (June-September) run off
in small earth dams. The topography and stream pattern for the project
area indicate a promising potential for earth dam construction. This is
further substantiated by the 1:50,000 topographical maps which are available
(with 50 and 25 foot contour intervals) and which indicate a large number
of potential locations for small dam sites.
6.        Hydrological data are generally poor, but enough for small dam
development purposes. Annual rainfall in the project area varies from
700 mm-1,200 mm, and a runoff yield varying from 0.080-0.150 M3/Km2 can
be expected. Evaporation from the natural water surface is very high.
being about 2,000 mm per year.
7.        A careful analysis of the topography, availability of local
construction materials and hydrology indicates that a network of small dams
of about 120,000 m3 in total capacity each, should be built. Each would
be fed by runoff from a catchment of 3 to 4 km2 in area and would be
capable of meeting the year-round demands of 2,000 people and their live-
stock. The dams would be equipped to provide a gravitational distribution
system for conveying water from the reservoir outlet to delivery points at
separate locations for people and livestock.
8.        A typical earthdam for the project area would have specifications
as detailed below:
(i) Features of average dam
-    Total maximum height                           6.70 m
-    Free board                                     1.50 m
-    Depth at spillway level                        5.2  m
-    Total capacity                            120,000  m3
-    Dead storage or sediment space             20,000  m3
-    Useful storage capacity                   100,000  m3



ANNEX 5
Page 3
The spillway overflow with concrete sills with rip-rap in
between (it may be necessary to peg down the rip-rap with
gabion wire in special cases) would be designed by the Soil
Conservation Engineer after he had surveyed the catchment,
studied its characteristics, rainfall intensity and worked
out the run-off.
Dam - earth-fill with clay core if necessary, having 4 m
top width and 1:2 to 1:3 side slopes with rip-rap on up-
stream face, containing about 7,000 m3 of fill.
Foundation - treated to impervious zone.  Core wall would be
installed to an impervious foundation.
Outlet work - outlet pipes of 7.5 cm in diameter laid in
concrete, with at least three concrete flanges, equipped
with a gate valve on the downstream.
Dam design - as portions of the project area are under soils
which contain a high percentage of clay and others under
deep soils containing high sand content, the design of each
dam would be based on the results of soil tests the proposed
borrow and foundation materials.
(ii) Distribution Sygstem
-    Main gravity pipeline - iron pipe of 7.5 cm in diameter
laid at 1:1,000 slope for a distance to clear valley bottom,
not exceeding 300 m.
-    Branch pipeline - iron of 5 cm in diameter conveying water
from the main pipe to the separate delivery points at a
distance of at least 50 m for villagers and 100 m for
livestock from the main pipe.
-    Villager delivery points - installed with an adequate
concrete platform and faucets. 1/
-    2 livestock delivery points - installed with an adequate
brick trough and faucets.
(iii)  Useful life of Tank:       20 years 2/
Design sediment space in tank: 1,200 x 20 x 0.85 = 20,000 m3
1/   The possibility of using hydrograms, for pumping water from the larger
dams to nearby villages should be considered as to cost and applicability.
2/   For this purpose, the first 20 years after completion of the tank are
considered as 'Productive life of the reservoir' and years beyond this
are considered as 'falling' or ' depleting' life.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
Total tank capacity:
-    Active or useful storage                   100,000 m3
-    Space for sediment deposit                  20,000 m3
-    Total capacity                              20,000 m
9.        The project could not in the first instance provide sufficient
dams to satisfy all project farmers. However, it would attempt to satisfy
demand in the more critical areas, and provide reserve capacity for most
village unit areas. 85 dams would be constructed over the 5 years of the
project; 5 would be built in year 1, followed bi 20 in each subsequent year.
Thus on average there would be one dam to 90 Ian which approximates to one
per 1200 farming families.
B. Soil Conservation Mfeasures
10.       Soil erosion occurs through overstocking and concentration of
stock at sparsely located watering points, and through poor cultivation
methods including badly aligned planting ridges and late planted crops
at low plant densities.
11.       Virtually no soil conservation measures have been undertaken on
a large scale in northern Nigeria and there are no practical specifications
based on past experience that can be followed. Consequently it would
remain for the project conservation engineer to modify the designs and
layouts as experience is gained during project implementation.
12.       Dam catchments would require special conservation measures to
prevent erosion and siltation. Conservation on other areas would be of a
less intensive nature but designed to provide a basis for any future more
intensive conservation measures that may be required. To avoid concentration
of livestock at the limited number of existing watering places and planned
project dams a number of tapkis (ponds) would be constructed.
13.       Dam Catchment Conservation Measures
(i) Master ridges would be marked with a plough on the contour
so that all cultivation would follow the contour, 3 lines
per 20 ha should be sufficient.
(ii) The Conservation Engineer would locate natural or artificial
water ways approximately 1,000 m apart. He would shape them
to the required size and if necessary have them grassed. In
the second year when the grass had established, 'vee' ditches
or cut-off drains would be constructed.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Specifications. The diversion ditches (cut-off drains) would be
approximatelv 1 ,000 m long draining both ways into the grassed waterway.
The ditches would have a cross sectional area of 0.8 m2 (37 cm deep and
3.6 m wide) and have a grade of 1:400 and be 250 m apart.
Equipment Required. 1 x 125 hp motor grader, 1 x 75 hp tractor
and plow.
14.       Conservation of Other Areas.  A conservation unit would be attached
to the road unit, and as the roads progressed master ridges would be
constructed with a plow over an area extending approximately 2 km each side
of the road. 3 master ridges per 20 ha would be sufficient. At the same
time lines for future access roads to villages or group of farms would be
marked, the natural drainage ways would also be demarcated so that they
would be left out of cultivation and allowed to revert to grass.  In addition
farmers should be encouraged to practice tie ridges (boxed ridges). The
ties would be 1 to 2 m apart and half the height of ridges. This would give
maximum absoption in the early light rains. In the heavy rains the ties
would break and allow the excess water to drain off. Ties would be replaced
towards the end of the rains to assist in improving the infiltration of the
late rains.
15.       Tapkis.  These would be constructed by a 140 hp angle dozer in
shallow depressions with a catchment area of 15 to 20 ha. Should the site
chosen have insufficient catchment, the catchment would be increased by the
use of diversion ditches.
llethod. The dozer would scoop out the earth over an area approx-
imately 40 m x 30 m with an average depth of 1.7 m.  The embankment would be
made around the lower end giving a small increase to the holding capacity of
the tapl;i.  The diversion ditches would b9 constructed by the motor grader
on a 1:500 grade with a capacity of 0.8 m  (37 cm deen and 3.6 m wide).
These would stop short of the tankl and be allowed to discharge towards a
bank 1 m high, 1 m thick and of sufficient length to allow the water to
circulate around the bank into the tapkis.  This would reduce the velocity
and cause the silt to be deposited outside the tapki.
Equipment. N1o additional equipment would be required for tapki
construction as construction by the conservation unit would be done during
the crop growing season. About 160 tapkis would be constructed during the
life of the project.

NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Organization, Staff Requirements and Training
A. Organization
1.        The project organization as described in Chapter VI of the main
report would assume responsibility for all existing functions of MANR within
the Funtua Administration Division, except for those aspects concerning animal
health, fisheries, forestry, and irrigation. The project organization would
follow the pattern as shown at Schedule I of this annex and would comprise
a project executive committee, primarily controlling policy, budgets and
audits, and an operational project management unit responsible for the day-
to-day operations involved in implementation of project policies. The project
executive committee and management unit would be established by a Govern-
gazetted order, a proposed draft for which is at Schedule II.
2.        The Funtua Project Management Unit (FPMU) would operate through
a number of sections; the head of each would be responsible to the Project
Manager. The sections would be: Administration, Finance, Technical Ser-
vices, Credit Services, Market Services, and Engineering Services.
3.        The Administration Section would be responsible for personnel ad-
ministration, maintenance of records, organisation of project transport,
office administration, project security and public relations.
4.        The Finance Section would be responsible for budgeting, budget
control, internal audit, procurement, stores, salary payments, accounting
procedures, cost accounting, etc. It would help in setting up financial
procedures for the revolving credit fund, and would conduct the internal
audit of the fund.
5.        The Technical Services Section would provide basic technical sup-
port and services to all those project actions designed to increase farmer
productivity including:
a.   Development Services would provide the coordinating link be-
tween marketing, credit and extension. The project would
have 5 development centers each serving a development area
and controlled by a Development Officer who would coordinate
all project activities within the area. Each area would cor-
respond to one of the five Administrative Districts in the
project area. Apart from coordination the Development Officer
would be directly responsible for extension services. The
latter would be organized on the basis of one Agricultural
Assistant (AA) for farm service center. In turn



ANNEX 6
Page 2
each AA would supervise a number of Agricultural Instructors
(AI) who would work at the hamlet level. The AA and AI would
provide basic extension advice to farmers and groups of farmers.
Associated with the extension service would be a small live-
stock advisory unit that would (i) identify livestock problems
and make recommendations for livestock programs within the
area; and (ii) provide advice on practical animal husbandry
to these farmers engaged in livestock operations sponsored
by the project. In this connection close liaison would be
maintained with MANR veterinary services.
b.   Seed Farms.  Two seed farms at Daudawa and Malumfashi would
be developed to provide part of project seed requirements in
conjunction with contract operations by some of the larger
farmers in nearby areas. The seed farms would also be used
for practical demonstration purposes, applied research and
training.
c.   Farm Management Advisory Service.  This would be a small unit
working closely with the seed farms, providing management
advice to larger "commercial" farmers within the project area.
These farmers would be directed to NAB and/or commercial banks
for farm credit and the unit would assist them in this.
d.   Tractor Hire Services, now supplied by MANR, would be continued
under the project, but would be phased out gradually in favor
of private contractors sponsored by the project.
e.   Training would be undertaken at the Daudawa training center,
the capacity of which would be expanded from 20 to 40 students,
and at a new center to be constructed at Malumfashi. The two
centers would be used initially for training and retraining
project staff in extension, credit, marketing, and other dis-
ciplines. As the project develops more of the available train-
ing capacity would be allocated to project area farmers.
6.        The Credit Services Section would be developed in parallel to the
development services and would be structured around the Development Centers
at District level, and at the farm service centers at farmer level. The
service would provide farmers with inputs on credit or for cash, assist in
recovery of repayments, and keep accounts. An area Credit Officer would
control credit activities within each development area, and each village
service center would have two Credit Assistants (Annex 9 gives details).
7.        The Market Services Section would be responsible for produce in-
spection at produce markets, for market liaison and information, and for the
control and monitoring of Licensed Buying Agents (LBA). It would also be
sary



ANNEX 6
Page 3
responsible for the development of group marketing systems, and where neces-
sary for linking producers directly with distant consumer, e.g. contracts
for maize supply to southern feed mill operators. Each Development Center
would have a marketing supervisor assisted by two market assistants. A
produce examiner would be posted to each farm service center to service
its market. Marketing services would be responsible for the logistics in-
volved in handling and distributing farm inputs. In this respect the pro-
duce examiners would act as storekeepers during the period when markets are
closed, and when the main handling and distribution of inputs would occur.
8.        The Engineering Services Section would be primarily responsible
for implementation of the physical infrastructural program for the project.
It would determine design and make specifications for buildings, houses,
offices, etc. prepare tender documents, evaluate bids, and supervise con-
tractors. It would be responsible for the procurement of all plant, vehicles
and equipment, and for their operation and maintenance. It would prepare
designs for roads and associated structures, dams, and soil conversation
works, and would ensure their construction according to plan. It would
operate a project workshop. Finally it would have a special land use section
to provide support for other project units as well as to provide information
directly relevant to civil works construction. In all instances it would
work closely with Ministry of Works Engineers.
B. Staff Requirements
9.        Details of the number of staff needed for the project, their
availability within the State and probable shortfalls are shown at Tables
1-3 of this Annex. Job descriptions and qualifications for the more senior
positions are at Schedule III. In general existing staff in the State have
received inadequate training and experience to permit them to effectively
manage and operate the project and therefore, internationally recruited
staff would be necessary for some of the key posts. Existing Funtua Division
staff would come under the project and additional staff would be transferred
to the project from other areas of the State. Priority during the first two
years would be given to the project for those Agricultural Assistants com-
pleting ongoing training courses. On this basis sufficient professional and
technical staff would be available. Junior technical staff, particularly
agricultural instructors (Al), would be recruited and trained by the project
at its own training centers. Such training would initially comprise of
simple short courses on specific crops and associated problems. The AI
would receive further training after a period of field experience, and there-
after regular refresher courses. Inexperienced credit and marketing staff
would be trained in a similar manner through a series of inservice training
courses.
10.       The Project Executive Committee on the advice of the Project Mana-
ger would be responsible for the appointment of all senior staff. Staff
appointments at lower levels would be the responsibility of the Project



ANNEX 6
Page 4
Iranagement Unit in consultation with relevant Government departments from
wvhiclh particular transfers might be made. Staff salaries are compatible
with existing Government scales, and would from time to time be reviewed
by the Project Executive Committee.
C. Staff Training
11.       Professional training is provided at universities throughout
Nigeria. The Ahmadu Bello University at Zaria in the North Central State
caters especially for the six northern States, although northern students
attend both Federal and State financed universities elsewhere in Nigeria,
and abroad. A degree in agriculture is given by ABU and the project would
expect to recruit some of its professional staff as they graduate from ABU.
12.       Diplomate (3 year course for holders of West African Schoo,l Cer-
tificate) and certificate (2 year) level training is the responsibility of
the Division of Agriculture and Livestock Service Training (DALST) of ABU.
Graduates of these courses are employed by the Ministries of Agriculture
and Natural Resources of the northern States.
13.       Four schools are operated by DALST:
a.   Samaru (North Central State)
Offering, at present, certificate courses in extension,
farm mechanics, home economics and a diploma course in
home economics. Expansion of this school, planned for
completion by 1975, would add diploma courses in farm
mechanics and extension to the syllabus.
b.   Balcura (North Western State)
Offering a certificate course in irrigation agronomy;
presently located at Samaru pending completion of the
new school at Bakura.
c.   Kaduna Livestock Service Training Center (North Central
State)
Offering certificate courses in livestock and range
management, and diploma courses in animal oroducts in-
spection, and range management.
d.   Kabba (Kwara State)
Offering. diploma courses in agriculture at present, and
certificate courses in agriculture from 1974.



ANNEX 6
Page 5
14.       Present enrollment at these schools is as follows:
1st Year        2nd Year

/1   1st year general course with opportunity to specialize in the second

/2   Soecial course to give existing Ministry technical staff the oppor-
tunity to upgrade to AS (1972 only).
/3   The capacity of these schools is being increased and it is forecast
that by 1975 Samaru would have a capacity for 600 students and the
other schools 300 students each.
15.       The output of diplomates (Agricultural Superintendents) and
certificate holders (Agricultural Assistants) bonded to work through the
terms of their scholarships for North Central State is forecast as follows:
1974 - 7; 1975 - 25: and 1976 - 31. These together with intra State trans-
fers would meet project requirements.
16.       The existing training of agricultural extension staff, graduates,
diplonates and certificate holders is sadly lacking in practical farm man-
agement, and the ability of senior staff in the field to give advice to
farmers directed at increasing profit margins needs to be greatly improved.
At present extension staff tend to give blanket advice without considering
economic irnnlications.  Training establishments, for both senior and junior
staff should improve the quality of their farm management training.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
17.       Farm training centers are operated by North Central State and
graduate Agricultural Instructors (AI), the lowest grade of extension work-
er. The State also has farm institutes for training farmers and farmers'
sons. Under the project it is proposed to upgrade the existing Daudawa
Farm Institute to a Farm Training Center, and to build a new Farm Training
Center at Ilalumfashi. These centers would be used to train, in the initial
years of the project, project staff particularly AI and Credit Assistants.
Later on the emphasis would be more on farmer training.
18.       The recruitment of individuals for training as agricultural in-
structors, Credit Assistants etc. should not cause too many problems since
the basic primary level of education is relatively good throughout northern
Nigeria, and there is an increasing surplus of West African School Certifi-
cate holders on the labor market.
19.       Staff recruited by the project would be expected to have certain
minimum qualifications: Agricultural Officers - degree in agriculture;
Agricultural Superintendents - diploma or upgraded from lower level; Agri-
cultural Assistant - certificate; Agricultural Instructor - primary seven;
Credit Officer - degree or diploma, or certificate in accountancy preferably
with some commercial bank experience; Credit Assistants - West African
School Certificate; Market Supervisor - degree holder or relevant experience
in market organization; Marketing Assistant - West African School Certifi-
cate; Produce Inspector - primary seven; Rural Engineers - engineering de-
gree.
20.       The terms and conditions of the emplovment of project personnel
would conform with those established for all employees of the State Govern-
ment except that professional and technical grade staff would be provided
housing as an incentive to live and work in remote areas, and that new
terms would be made for both expatriate and Nigerian contract staff. For
expatriate staff contract terms would take into account the international
market for such staff and the benefits that normally accrue to such appoint-
ments. For Nigerian staff contract terms would provide similar benefits as
normal pensionable Government staff but would also provide for a gratuity
(to be determined bv FPEC) in lieu of a pension.
21.       Management Training.  In addition to technical staff, training
project management would play an important role in training staff to fill
managerial positions. In this respect internationally recruited staff would,
as one of their responsibilities, ensure the adequate training of their
subordinates, and would be required to submit to FPEC through the project
manager, quarterly reports on the training aspects of their section. The project
manager would have the overall responsibility for ensuring that an adequate mana-
gement training program is maintained and would report regularly on its
progress. There are sufficient funds available for the recruitment of a
training specialist if the project manager so requires.

ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Establishment of Project Management Unit
1.        As a condition of loan effectiveness the North Central State
Executive Council would be required to publish an "order" in the State
Government Gazet : establishing the Funtua Project Management Unit (FPMU)
and the Funtua Project Executive Committee (FPEC). The order would set out
the objectives and responsibilities of FPMU and FPEC, and would include the
following.
2.        The FPMU would be managed by a Project Manager and its objectives
should be:
(a) development of Funtua Administrative Division with a view
to increasing production of agriculture and raising the
living standards of the rural population;
(b) construction of roads, water supplies and soil conservation
works associated with agricultural development;
(c) development and management of seed multiplication farms;
(d) training of project staff and farmers;
(e) provision of village service centers and stores;
(f) provision of extension, credit and marketing services;
(g) procurement of farm Inputs; and
(h)  development of supporting infrastructure such as houses,
buildings, offices etc. for the above services.
3.        The FPMU would be controlled by the Funtua Project Executive
Committee (FPEC) which would be made up as follows:



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 2
(a)  Permanent Secretary            -    Ministry of Agriculture and
Natural Resources.
(b)  Representative                 -    Ministry of Finance.
(c)  Representative                 -    Ministry of Local Government
(d)  Representative                 -    Ministry of Transport and Works
(e)  Representative                 -    Ministry of Economic Planning
(f)  Representative                 -    NCS Water Board
(g)  Representative                 -    North Central State Works Board
(h)  Director of Federal Agricultural Department or representative
(i) Chief Agricultural Officer, for the North Central State
(j) Two members from the project area to be appointed by the
Military Governor
The Project Manager would be the Secretary of the FPEC.
4.        FPEC would be responsible to the Commissioner of Agriculture and
would have the following responsibilities:
(a)  Policy.   FPEC would determine agricultural and development
policy, and would consult with the Federal Government and
the World Bank when any major changes in policy may be
required;
(b) Finance.
(i) FPEC would approve the annual budget and would ensure
that sufficient Government funds are readily available
to the project;
(ii) FPEC would appoint independent auditors;
(iii) FPEC would submit audited statement of accounts to the
Commissioner of Agriculture within four months of the
closure of each financial year;
(iv) FPEC would approve the opening of project bank accounts;
and
(v) the Chairman of FPEC would approve the monthly cash flow
statements submitted by the manager of FPMU;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 3
(vi)   approve annually the terms of lending to project farmers
on the basis of recommendations made by FPMU;
(c) Staff and Personnel Matters. In consultation with the Manager
of FPMU, FPEC would be responsible for the appointment of all
senior project staff and employees, their terms and conditions
of employment and annual review of salaries and allowances;
(d) Award of Contracts. FPEC would on the advice of the Manager
of FPMU be responsible for approval of any single contract
valued at more than N 30,000;
(e) Inter-Lepartmental Coordination. The FPEC through its members
would be responsible for the coordination at Ministry level
for those issues that may effect the proper development of
the project.
Meetings of FPEC
S.    (a)  The Chairman would preside over all the meetings of the FPEC
which he attends, and in his absence the present members would
appoint one of their number to preside over such a meeting.
(b) The Chairman of the FPEC would invite the FPEC to meet at such
time and place as he may think suitable; provided that no more
than three months would elapse between any meeting and the
next. In the absence of the Chairman, the Manager would call
the FPEC to meet whenever he deems this necessary.
(c) Six members of the FPEC would constitute a quorum of any meeting
of the FPEC. The FPEC would pass its decisions by the Majority
of the votes of its members present and voting, and in case of
equality of votes the Chairman would have a casting vote.
(d) The FPEC may call any person to attend any of its meetings
without that person having a counted vote.
6.         FPMU would be responsible to FPEC for the day to day operations of
the project. In particular it would be responsible for the carrying out of
the objectives as stated in para 2 above. FPMU would be managed by a Project
Manager who would report directly to the Chairman of FPEC. The main duties
of FPMU would be:



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 4
(a) the implementation of an agricultural development program
within Funtua Administration Division following the policy
guidelines of the FPEC;
(b) the control and operation of the project revolving credit fund;
(c) the preparation of annual budgets and cash flows for approval
by FPEC. In particular FPMU would submit to the Chairman of
the FPEC monthly cash flow statements indicating any changes
in requirements for State Government funds due to changes in
project expenditures and inflow anticipated from the Federal
Government. Unless informed to the contrary within 5 days of
dispatch of such statements to the Chairman of the FPEC, the
manager would be authorized to make the proposed expenditures.
(d) the recruitment of project staff, in consultation, when
necessary with other Government Departments, and for senior
staff, with approval of FPEC;
(e) the advertising, negotiating, calling for tenders, and after
approval from FPEC, for concluding such contracts with persons
and organizations either inside or outside the State for the
erection of buildings, the construction of civil works and
the supply of goods, services and materials as may seem to be
requisite for the proper discharge of its duties;
(f) the operation of project bank accounts;
(g) the preparation of reimbursement claims against project
expenditure, for submission to the Federal Government and
the World Bank.
7.         FPMU would administer a field evaluation unit that would be
technically directed by a Federal Government controlled Project Monitoring
and Evaluation Unit (PMEU).
8.         FPMU would prepare quarterly and annual reports for the simultaneous
submission to FPEC, North Central State Executive Council, Federal Government
of Nigeria and the World Bank.
9.         FPMU would be delegated any additional duties and powers by FPEC
to ensure the attainment of the objectives as set out in this order.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
JOB DESCRIPTIONS AND QUALIFICATIONS OF SENIOR STAFF
PROJECT MANAGER
Experience would be the main criteria for the selection of a Project
Manager, although a formal qualification in agriculture would be important.
At least 10 years' experience in agricultural development work in
the field, including development orientated projects, preferably in Africa,
would be required. A high degree of administrative ability, an experience in
dealing with the highest level of Government, especially in committee work,
and previous involvement in extension in small farmer communities would all
be necessary.
The responsibilities of the Project Manager would be the overall
and complete control of all project activities and the coordination of all
project activities with those of other Federal and State Government agencies
and local authorities.
His duties would include:
(a) control of all project funds with the assistance
of the Chief Accountant;
(b) establishment of a cost analysis and record keeping
system which would enable project progress and
achievement to be monitored and a meaningful eval-
uation of the project's impact possible;
(c) responsibility for project personnel matters with
the assistance of the Chief Administrative Officer;
(d) co-ordination of all project activities;
(e) reporting project progress to Government through
written reports and through the various project
committees; and
(f) training project staff.
The project Manager would be responsible to the Chairman of the
Funtua Project Executive Committee (the Chairman being the Permanent Secre-
tary of the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources).



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 2
CHIEF ADMINISTRATIVE OFFICER
The Chief Administrative Officer would be a very senior civil
servant; or have had equivalent experience in a major public corporation or
private company.
In previous employment he would have undertaken senior administra-
tive responsibility, and have gained a thorough understanding of government
procedures.
His duties would include:
(a) responsibility for all project personnel;
(b) project transportation;
(c) maintenance of a project registry;
(d) the physical control and checking of project stores;
(e)  the general project security;
(f) the public relations aspects of the project; and
(g) for training administration staff.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
CHIEF ACCOUNTANT
The Chief Accountant would be a professionally qualified account-
ant with additional training in business management. He should have had at
least 10 years' experience in a senior position in a large public corpora-
tion or commercial company. If possible, he would have a knowledge of bank-
ing and credit procedures. Previous employment involving working with high
ranking government officials would be an advantage.
His main responsibility would be the control of all project fi-
nances on behalf of the Project Manager.  His duties would include:
(a) preparation of budgets, cash flows and estimates
of expenditure for the project;
(b) ensuring correct application for project funds;
(c) assisting the Credit Manager in establishing an
efficient and meaningful system of recording
agricultural credit transactions;
(d) the responsibilities for project stores and
requisites, with the assistance of the section
and department heads, and for stores control
and audit procedures



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 3
(e) internal auditing of project accounts; and
(f) training accounting staff.
CHIEF TECHNICAL OFFICER
The Chief Technical Officer would be a qualified Agriculturalist.
He would have a thorough knowledge of tropical crop production and related
problems.
He would have had at least 5 years experience in extension work
with small farmers where cotton, groundnuts and cereals were part of the
cropping system.
He would be responsible for all technical aspects of the project
and his duties specifically would include:
(a) organizing research, and seed multiplication and
distribution , with the assistance of the Seeds
Officer and Farm Managers;
(b) supervision of the training program for all exten-
sion staff;
(c) organizing and ensuring the effectiveness of exten-
sion services in the project area;
(d) supervising the Livestock Advisor, the Farm machin-
ery Section and the Farm Management Advisor; and
(e) liaising with and assisting the Market Section to
to ensure it meets the projects' requirements in
marketing.
Hle would be directly responsible to the Project lManager, and would
assist the Project Manager in his dealings with other government and local
authority agencies in matters related to the Technical Services.
CHIEF ENGINEER
The Chief Engineer should be a qualified civil engineer. Some
training in mechanical engineering would be an added advantage.
He would have had at least 5 years' experience in the problems of
rural engineering in developing countries. A sound knowledge and experience
in road, water, and soil conservation engineering would he considered essen-
tial. He would have had senior administrative responsibility in the engin-
eering field and would be accustomed to working with government agencies.
His duties would include;
(a) control of all project engineering activities;
(b) budgetary control;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 4
(c)  the preparation of all tenders, contracts, and
quantity surveys;
(d)  supervision of land use planning, and the design
of project roads, buildings and dams; and
(e)  for the efficient coordination of the different
units of the Project engineering services, and
closely liaison with those other Government depart-
ments, such as the Ministry of Works and the
Ministry of Local Government, who may be asso-
ciated with the project.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
CREDIT MANAGER
The Credit Manager would be a professionally qualified accountant
with a demonstrable sound working knowledge of the establishment and cbr-
poration of rural credit institutions. He would have at least 5 years of
experience in responsible positions in smallholder rural credit admini8tra-
tion, and have a record of sound administrative ability. Experience in the
formation of farmers' credit groups or societies and working with these or-
ganizations, and/or cooperatives, would be an advantage.
His prime responsibility would be the control of project farmer
credit operation; which would involve initially the disbursement of loans in
kind and their recovery.
His responsibilities would include:
(a)  formation of a Revolving Credit Fund as out-
lined in the project appraisal report;
(b)  establishing an efficient accounting and re-
cording system for farmers' loans;
(c)  requisitioning of funds and other required in-
puts for timely disbursement and supply to
farmers;
(d)  liaison with the Evaluation Unit to provide data
and information needed in project evaluation; and
(e)  training of credit staff.
The Credit Manager would be responsible to the Project Manager.
CHIEF MARKETING OFFICER
Senior management responsibility in a large marketing organization
would be the principal qualification of the Chief Marketing Officer.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 5
He would have experience in the production, primary marketing and
selling of tropical crops, particularly cotton, groundnuts and maize.
He would be responsible for the project's Mlarketing Services, and
his duties would include:
(a) monitoring and controlling the operations of all
licensed buying agents operating in the project
area;
(b) establishing a market information service;
(c) liaison with the statutory marketing boards and
other organizations private and public, to
ensure the effective marketing and smooth flow
of produce from the project area; and
(d) co-ordinating with the Credit Manager to estab-
lish a system of credit collection at time of
crop marketing.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
SENIOR FARM MANAGER
The Senior Farm rfanager would have some formal training in Agri-
culture, but more importantly would have had at least 10 years' successful
experience in managing large commercially oriented farms in the tropics.
A specific experience of cotton, groundnuts, sorghum and maize production,
or at least two of these crops would be required. Previous experience in
growing these and other crops for seed production would be helpful.  A sound
knowledge of farm recording and accounting procedures would be essential.
The Senior farm manager would manage one of the project farms and
would through another manager be responsible for the second project farm.
In total he would be responsible for the efficient operation of a 400 ha
unit growing cotton, groundnuts and cereals for seed.
Ilis duties would include:
(a) budget preparation and cash flow control for
the farm;
(b) preparation and implementation of a cropping
plan for the farm;
(c) establishment, maintenance and operating of a
suitable seed dressing and cleaning unit for
the farm;
(d) procuring, maintaining, and operating appro-
priate farm machinery;
(e) control of farm labor;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 6
(f) assisting the Farm Management Advisor in pro-
viding advice and assisting the large privately
owned farm units of the project area, especial-
ly those growing seed on contract;
(g) liaison with the Seeds Officer on technical seed
production matters; and
(h) training junior staff in practical large scale
farming.
He would be responsible to the Chief Technical Officer.
SEEDS OFFICER
The Seeds Officer would have had formal training in Agriculture or
Agricultural Botany. He would have had specific experience in commercial
large scale seed production, preferably with at least two of the following:
cotton, groundnuts, maize, and sorghum. He would advise the Managers of the
Seed Multiplication Farms on seed production and would be responsible for
ensuring the distribution of improved seeds to farmers in the project area.
His duties would include:
(a) advising and assisting the Farm Managers in pro-
curing and operating suitable seed cleaning and
dressing units;
(b) sponsoring progressive farmers in seed production
and thereafter for advising them in seed production
techniques;
(c) the building up of a viable seed industry in the
project area; and
(d) the training of technical and professional pro-
ject personnel in seed production and distribution.
He would be responsible to the Chief Technical Officer.
FARM MANAGEMENT ADVISOR
The Farm Management Advisor should be a fully qualified agricul-
turalist specialized in Farm Management. Experience in Farm Mtanagement in
tropical agriculture would be an advantage. He would be responsible, under
the direction of the Chief Technical Officer, for all Farm Management Ex-
tension work within the project, such work would be directed towards the
needs of the larger farmers of the project area.
Hlis duties would include:
(a) assisting larger farmers within the project
area by preparing farm plans, cropping systems,
budgets, cash flows, loan applications and any
other similar assistance for the efficient op-
eration of their farns;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 7
(b) assisting the farm managers in preparing farm
plans, budgets, cash flows and any other neces-
sary records for the Seed Mlultiplication Farms;
(c) training project staff in Farm Management methods;
and
(d) assisting the Evaluation Unit in data collection.
LIVESTOCK ADVISOR
The Livestock Advisor would have formal training in Veterinary
Science or Animal Husbandry, and would have had experience in Animal Hus-
bandry work in tropical countries.
He would be responsible for the preparation of a long term plan
for livestock development within the project area. This would be based on
an analysis of the potential for on-farm systems of livestock and associated
feed production, and inter alia would be aimed at the evolvement of systems
to make better use of communal grazing lands.
LAND USE PLAINMING OFFICER
The Land Use Planning Officer would have had formal training in
agricultural, soil science or land resource use; land surveying and photo-
grammetry. Previous experience in Land Resource work and associated land
and agricultural development planning would be essential.
Duties of the Land Use Planning Officer would be to:
(a) prepare specifications for the production of
aerial photographs and uncontrolled mozaics
of the project area;
(b) undertake a complete photo analysis of the
project area and prepare land use resource maps
and inventory of the area;
(c) plan, in conjunction with the Chief Technical
Officer, the location of roads and dams to be
constructed under the project;
(d) prepare all drawings etc. of dam, soil conserva-
tion and road designs that may be submitted by
the engineering sections of the project;
(e) undertake any such other mapping and surveying
considered necessary by the Chief Engineer;
(f) undertake photoanalytical work that may be re-
quired by the evaluation unit or Chief Technical
Officer;
(g) give general guidance on Land Resource use to
Engineering and Technical Services staff; and
(h) training Nigerian staff in land use and asso-
ciated teclniques.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 8
He would be directly responsible to the Chief Engineer.
SOIL AND WATER CONSERVATION ENGINEER
The Soil and W4ater Conservation Engineer would have had formal
training in engineering, preferably related to dam construction and soil con-
servation. He would have had at least 5 years' experience in earth dam con-
struction and soil conservation in Africa. He would be responsible for over-
all supervision of the dam construction and soil conservation component of
the project. His responsibilities would include:
(a) survey and design of water catchment areas and dams;
(b) design planning, and supervision of construction of
all soil conservation work;
(c) liaison with the Road Engineer and Land Use Planning
Officer in integrating dam construction and soil con-
servation works into a general land use plan and the
road building program; and
(d) training project staff in dam construction and soil
conservation work.
The Soil and Water Conservation Engineer would be responsible to
the Chief Engineer.
'[ECYHICAL ENGINEER
The 'Mechanical Engineer would be a professionally qualified engin-
eer with specific experience in operating and maintaining heavy earth moving
machinery. His principal duties and responsibilities would be supervising
the operation and maintenance of all project plant, equipment, and vehicles.
His duties would include:
(a) establishment of a workshop capable of maintain-
ing project plant, equipment and vehicles;
(b) ordering and maintaining an appropriate supply
of stores and spare parts;
(c) ordering and maintaining an adequate and suitable
fuel supply; and
(d)  training of Nigerian mechanical engineers.
Ile would be directly responsible to the Chief Engineer.
ROAD ENGINEER
Successful experience in the design, construction and maintenance
of earth roads in tropical climates would be the main qualification of the
Road Engineer.  The Road Engineer would be responsible for implementing the
project road program. IHis duties would include:



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 9
(a)  advising the Chief Engineer on road design,
drainage and water disposal, culverts and river
crossing designs;
(b) the direction of all machinery and equipment put
at hiis disposal for construction purposes;
(c) the preparation and maintenance of records
adequate to enable unit costs to be established;
(d)  evaluation of road construction program and tech-
niques used; and
(e) training Nigerian engineers in road design and
construction methods.
Head PMEU Evaluation Section
The Head would be responsible for the operations of the
Evaluation Section of the Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit
(PMEU), and would direct the individual project evaluation programs
for the Funtua, Gombe and Gusau projects. He would be responsible
to the Director of the Federal Department of Agriculture.
The post would require advanced post graduate training, preferably
a PhD in agricultural economics, as well as considerable field experience
involving economic and social survev work in Africa. Total field experience
should not be less than five years.  His duties would include:
(a) preparing and controlling the budgets of PMEU's
evaluation section;
(h)  preparing a comprehensive evaluation program
based on a scientifically designed sampling
system;
(c) administrating the nrogram and its staff;
(d) controlling and supervising the field evalua-
tion teams;
(e) analysis of collected field data; and
(f)  production of evaluation reports for the
Governments and other institutions concerned.
SENIOR RESEARCHI OFFICERS
Two senior officers would support the Hlead of PMEU's evaluation
section.  Both positions would be filled by researchers with post-graduate
qualifications, nreferably PhD. One post would be filled by an experienced
statistician well versed in the technical aspects of sampling surveying;
development of svstems suitable for computer programming, and for the analysis



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 10
of results. The other post would be filled by a rural sociologist experienced
in surveys and evaluation. Such a man would have considerable field experience
in Africa. The Senior research officers would be expected to train the more
junior staff in their disciplines. In particular, they would be required to
assist the Director in formulation and subsequent modification as desired of
the five year evaluation program.
SENIOR EVALUATION OFFICFR
A senior evaluation officer would be required to direct the field
enumeration units attached to the individual project. This job would be
essentially to supervise enumerators, implement programs set out by the Head
of PMEU's evaluation section, and feed back the required information for
analysis. His views and suggestions would be taken into account during the
programming of the enumeration process and any subsequent amendments to it.
The senior evaluation officer would be technically responsible to the Head of
PMEU evaluation section, and administratively responsible to the project, manager.
The senior evaluation officer would have post graduate training
in agricultural economics, or rural economics: and would have controlled and
directed field enumeration units.

ANNEX 7
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Inputs
1.         The traditional agriculture of the Funtua area uses almost no
modern agricultural inputs. Currently there is very little demand for, and
very little supply of, such inputs as fertilizers, insecticides, sprayers
and other farm machinery. Yet, if the project is to achieve its goals, the
project must make available a supply of these inputs, create a demand for
them, develop the means for farmers to acquire them, and educate farmers in
their use - even supervise their use where necessary to assure results.
2.         At present suppliers of farm inputs in the project area are either
absent or inadequate. Therefore, the project would be an importer and re-
tailer of critical farm supplies and equipment. These supplies include
1,800 m tons fertilizer in year one, increasing to 22,370 m tons in year five;
4,000 sprayer kits in year one increasing to 14,000 in year five; as well as
ox drawn equipment, seeds, and insecticides. The logistics of acquiring,
moving and distributing these quantities would be substantial and would be
the responsibility of the Chief Marketing Officer. The selling price of
inputs would be based on an ex-farm center store cost.
3.         Inputs would be transported to the project center at Funtua where
a 4,000-ton storage facility would be constructed at Funtua railway yard and
to 77 village stores each with a 500-ton storage capacity. Rail to village
store transportation would be arranged with private truckers, many of whom
would be LBA transport contractors who would welcome back haul loads while
moving produce from village to market.
4.         Seasonal inputs include fertilizers, insecticides, seeds and bat-
tery sets for ULV sprayers. Fertilizers would be purchased in 50 kg bags
clearly marked to distinguish kind. The landed cost per m ton in the village
store (including a 10% handling charge) is estimated at N 196.40 for calcium
ammonium nitrate, N 183.20 for single super phosphate, and N 113.80 for muriate
of potash. Insecticides for cotton and cowpeas would cost farmers an esti-
mated N 1.65 per kilogram and for maize about N 0.33 per kilogram. Battery
sets for sprayers would cost about N 2.64 per set. Improved seeds would be
made available at varying costs.
5.         Subsidies.  NCS would continue to subsidize nitrogenous and phos-
phatic fertilizers to an amount of 50% of cif village store cost. At present
farmers pay N 40 per m ton for ammonium sulphate and N 32 per m ton for sin-
gle superphosphate (less than projected cif Nigerian ports price). To date,
muriate of potash has hardly been used in northern Nigeria. It has not been



ANNEX 7
Page 2
subsidized, and would not be under the project. Conversely it has been
Government policy to subsidize cotton insecticides. In the expectations of
increasing yields by as much as 400% under the project through spraying, and
associated practices, and because a large number of farmers would be involved,
compared to the negligible number at present, it is considered desirable that
cotton insecticides would not be subsidized. The subsidy element for fer-
tilizers is shown in Table 1.
6.         Sales of Seasonal Inputs.  It is expected that 25% of seasonal in-
puts would be sold for cash at farm service centers. Payments would be
made at the service center, recorded by cash register, and simultaneously
entered in the stores register. The remaining 75% of seasonal inputs would
be sold on credit. The credit system is described in more detail in Annex 8.
Sales on credit would be in kind and would be similar to the system for a
cash sale except that they would be registered on the farmer's credit card.
Tables 2, 3, and 4 show the amounts involved.
7.         Medium Term Inputs and Farm Equipment include ULV sprayers, ox
drawn equipment kits and tractor kits. These would be procured by the pro-
ject for sale to farmers. ULV sprayers together with protective gloves and
face masks would be sold for N 14.9. An estimated 47,000 sprayer kits would
be made available to farmers over the 5 year development period (Table 5).
Ox drawn kit at N 165 each would include an ox cart, plow and ridger. An
estimated 10,000 ox drawn kits are expected to be taken up over 5 years in
the project area (Table 5); mainly to larger farmers owning at least 6-10 ha
of land under cultivation. The project would not supply oxen. Farmers in-
terested in obtaining ox equipment would be expected to acquire oxen with their
own resources. Extreme difficulties are involved in supplying oxen on credit,
given their relatively high value and the opportunity of realizing this
through sale for meat.
8.         Tractor and Equipment  At present the Government supports a limited
but totally uneconomic tractor hire service within the project area. This
service would be phased out during the project period and would be replaced
by contract tractor operations provided by private entrepreneurs receiving
credit under the project. About 100 such loans would be made initially, and
would be made primarily to existing transport contractors, small businessmen
or larger farmers. The project would provide such contractors with maintenance
services, through its mechanical workshop and field servicing units, short
training courses for tractor drivers, and would direct operators to selected
areas. The tractor kit would include a 65 hp tractor, trailer, chisel plow,
disc harrow and a ridger. The project management would select a standard make
and specifications for the kit, taking into account prices obtained through
domestic competitive bidding, which would require a minimum level of local
maintenance service to be provided by the tractor suppliers. Project management
would help to establish realistic and fair hire rates for contractors. By the
fifth year of the project, tractor cultivations could be extended some 6,000
to 8,000 ha annually and if successful could be further expanded.

ANNEX 8

NIGERIA
NORTH CENTRAL STATE
FUNTUA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Credit
1.         Provision of inputs, and of technical services, is of little avail
unless farmers have the means for acquiring inputs. Thus farm credit plays
a critical role in the package of goods and services needed to make the
agriculture of a region more productive.
2.         Almost no commercial credit is available at present to farmers in
the project area, especially to the large majority who are small farmers.
Although small farmers apparently get some credit from local traders and
other private persons, it is unreliable, costly, and totally inadequate for
generating a more productive agriculture.  Commercial banks in the area are
not equipped to extend large numbers of small loans to farmers. The costs
of doing such business are prohibitative. Cooperative societies that have
extended credit to farmer members have failed more often than they have suc-
ceeded, partly because the inputs necessary to make enterprises productive
were not available, partly because no technical service or supervision ac-
companied the loan, and partly because of inadequate management of the co-
operatives themselves. In any case, sources of credit currently are total-
ly inadequate to serve the needs for an increasingly productive agricultural
economy.
Amounts of Credit Needed
3.         The credit system visualized for the project area would be limited
to providing farmers with specific inputs, namely fertilizers, insecticides,
seeds, sprayers, ox drawn equipment and a small amount of tractor equipment.
The estimated amounts of credit sales for each year of the 5-year project
period are:
Year 1   Year 2   Year 3    Year 4    Year 5
- *000
Fertilizers                      127.8    319.2      740.9    1,221.7   1,601.5
Insecticides, seeds, and
battery sets                95.9    313.1     691.5    1,037.9   1,258.5
Sprayers, oxdrawn equipment
and tractor equipment       86.0    222.1     511.9       715.7     904.6
Total                309.7    854.4   1,944.3    2,975.3   3,764.6



ANNEX 8
Page 2
4.        To lend and collect these sums effectively and efficiently it
would be necessary and preferable to establish an organization that would
be a self-perpetuating institution capable of serving the credit needs of
farmers in the area for an indefinite period of time.
5.        Operating Procedures for FPMU revolving credit fund.  Several
operating procedures lend themselves to adoption by FPMU in so far as they
would assure good service to customers at reasonable cost. The following
procedures would be those which FPMU would employ initially and modify as
required in the course of the projects' life. At first, lending would be
limited to input items carried by project farm service center stores; such
lending would be in amounts specified on a simple farm management plan approved
by projects' Development Service AA. A simple form of loan application would
be employed which would serve as a farm management plan and promisory note
at the same time.
6.        The Credit Assistant would calculate the farmers credit require-
ments and after clearance on the borrowers credit worthiness from the village
head the borrower would be authorized to present his application to the vil-
lage service center. The items specified would be provided to the farmer and
the sale recorded by a bookkeeping machine. At market time the farm service
center storekeeper would make it as convenient as possible for the borrower
to repay his loan.
7.        Each borrower would have a laminated identity card containing his
picture, serial number (which would identify his village and hamlet) and sig-
nature or mark for convenient identification and record keeping.
8.        Seasonal loans and two-year sprayer loans involving a total out-
standing credit of less than N 100 to borrowers in goodstanding would be
approved by Credit Assistants at the village level; attention being paid to
ensure that the time between loan application and the borrower actually ob-
taining his input items would be minimal. Larger loans and loans for oxdrawn
equipment would need to be approved by the relevant Credit Officer. Loans
of over N 500 and all tractor loans would require the Credit Manager's spe-
cific approval.
9.        FPMU *¢ould lend up to a maximum of N/5000.to any one borrower.  Larger
borrowers would Le directed to NAB or commercial banks.
10.       Group Obligation.  The procedures would provide that each borrower's
loan application be approved by the hamlet or village head, or a farmers'
association approved by the village authorities. Such approval would certify
to the applicants credit worthiness. Once each year, FPMU would summarize
all accounts of the hamlet or village. If defaults were found, the headman
would be asked to assist in collections. FPMU and the CA would be empowered
to withhold lending to anyone in that village or hamlet until defaults are
removed unless due cause for default is presented and accepted by the FPMU
The group obligation would be a powerful influence to avoid default under
social structure pertaining to northern Nigeria.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
11.        Provision would be made to extend credit to groups, or to village
farmers associations, if farmers wished to pool their needs for inputs and
to assume joint liability for the credit extended.
Terms of Lending
12.        Lending terms to farmers would be established each year prior to
the upcoming season. Preferably these terms should be established well in
advance, and certainly no later than the preceeding November. The lending terms
would be determined by the FPMUJ management for approval by FPEC after consul-
tation with relevant Federal Authorities and the World Bank. Interest rates
would vary between 5-9-1/2% depending on the viability of the input package
provided to the borrowers. Most appropriately it should be applied as follows:
-    seasonal loans for farm inputs -- repayable within 12 months
at a flat service charge rate of 10%. No downpayments required;
-    sprayer loans repayable within two years in annual equal
installments at a flat service charge rate of 10% per annum.
No downpayment required;
-    oxdrawn equipment loans within three years in three annual
equal installments at a flat service charge rate of 10%
per annum. A 20% downpayment required;
-    tractors and tractor drawn equipment within four years in
four annual equal installments at a flat service charge
rate of 10% per annum. A 30% downpayment required.
13.        The above terms may differ at the time of implementation depending
on the economic situation prevailing at the time. However, on present forecasts
and estimates, see farm budgets at Annex 1, the proposed crop improvements
and input packages could easily carry the terms proposed in para 12. It is
strongly recommended, for ease of administration and appreciation by the
borrowers, that a flat service rate made up of interest and services is applied
to all loans.
14.        During the implementation of the project, management and PMEU would
make a thorough study of various lending systems, such as cooperatives etc.,
that exist in the project area, with a view to making firm recommendations for
the conversion, in the fourth or fifth year of the project, of the revolving
credit fund into a form that can continue lending to the farmers after the
project development period is completed. It is therefore of great importance
that from the start of the project the revolving credit fund is operated on a
practical and commercial basis, and for this reason an illustrative cash flow
for the fund is shown at Table 1 of this Annex.
